PEOPLE'S REPUBLIC OF THE CONGO
APPRAISAL OF A SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
SUMMARY
i.        This report appraises a project covering the 1974-78 Investment
Plan of the Agence Transcongolaise des Communications (ATC) with the objec-
tives of improving ATC's operations, reducing costs, and enabling ATC to
satisfy traffic demands which originate in a large area of Equatorial Africa.
On the national level, the proposed project will contribute greatly to the
country's real growth and export earnings by ensuring the transport capacity
necessary for development of the almost unexploited rich forest reserves in
the Sangha and Oubangui river basins. Furthermore, the additional labor
involved in the expansion of logging operations could be an important factor
in slowing down migration to urban centers.
ii.       ATC, formed in 1969, is a Congo national agency which owns and
operates an integrated system comprising: (a) the Atlantic Ocean port of
Pointe Noire; (b) a railway, Chemin de Per Congo Ocean (CFCO), from Pointe
Noire to Brazzaville (510 km); and (c) the river ports (including Brazzaville)
and services on the Congo-Oubangui-Sangha rivers. The ATC system is the back-
bone of Congo's transport infrastructure and carries about 90% of all land
traffic. A major transport link connected to the CFCO is the 285 km railway
owned and operated by the Gabonese Compagnie Miniere de l'Ogooue (COMILOG)
for evacuation of manganese ore. ATC also provides a transport service for
parts of neighboring Chad, CAR, Cameroon, and Gabon.
iii.      The Congo road network totals about 10,800 km, of which only 3,000 km
provide all-weather service. Bank Group lending for highway development
comprises three Credits in 1969, 1970 and 1971 for a total of US$6.1 million.
Although transport coordination, which is the responsibility of the Ministry
of Public Works and Transport (MPWT), has not been an issue in the past due
to the monopoly situation of the ATC system, it will become more important
once the Pointe Noire-Brazzaville-Ouesso road axis parallel to much of the
ATC system, is fully developed. Following the Government's instructions to
all Ministries in 1975 to set up individual planning units, MPWT has already
designated a director for its unit which will be responsible for ensuring
a proper role for road and rail services.
iv.       The present ATC system was developed during the 1920's and 1930's
to cover the relatively simple transport requirements of the slowly emerging
economies of Central Africa. Over the last two decades, the pace of develop-
ment has quickened with a consequent growth in traffic demand. This demand
has heavily taxed ATC's capacity, and brought about a serious deterioration of
some parts of its physical assets. The proposed project, covering ATC's 1974-
78 Investment Plan, together with ATC's investment program to 1985, is designed
to ensure the continued functioning of the ATC system, render operations more
efficient, and provide for capacity expansion. A large part of the investment



- ii -
plan is allocated to the CFCO where immediate action is needed to avoid major
line interruptions threatened by failure of the track and structures over the
mountainous Holle-Loubomo section.
v.        Consultants recommended in a UNDP-financed feasibility study of
1971/72 that about 106 km of the CFCO line between Holle and Loubomo be re-
aligned, as the alternative of staged improvement of the existing alignment
was found to be impractical and uneconomical. The Bank Group subsequently
financed detailed engineering of the proposed realignment under its First
Railway Project (Credit 297-COB, US$6.3 million, April 1972, increased to
US$6.9 million, July 1973) which also provided for a traffic forecasting and
costing study, and for the purchase of 247 freight cars.
vi.       ATC's 1974-78 Investment Plan is expected to cost (net of taxes) about
US$233.8 million equivalent including physical and price contingencies, with
foreign exchange costs estimated at US$147.5 million. The Plan comprises the
following main items: (a) ATC Headquarters: improvement of training
facilities and programs (US$5.5 million); (b) Pointe Noire Port: provision of
an additional stacking area for timber handling, construction of new manganese
and general cargo berths, and dredging (US$12.8 million); (c) River Services:
procurement of mechanical handling equipment and additional floating craft,
expansion of Brazzaville workshops, and deepening of river channels
(US$21.1 million); and (d) CFCO Railway: procurement and renewal of locomo-
tives and rolling stock, track renewal, installation of signalling systems,
and expansion of workshops (US$45.6 million), and realignment of 88 km and
improvement of 18 km of the Holle-Loubomo section (US$148.8 million).
vii.      The largest item in the Investment Plan is the CFCO realignment.
The costs of the proposed works are covered under financing arrangements and
in amounts converted into US$ equivalent at current exchange rates as follows:
(i) by joint financing from the Bank (US$31.0 million), FED (US$17.3 million),
FAC (US$11.1 million), CCCE (US$3.3 million), and the Government (US$9.1 mil-
lion); and (ii) by tied or parallel financing from KDF/SFD/BADEA (US$43.6
million), ADB (US$12.4 million), CIDA (US$12.7 million), and CCCE (US$1.1 mil-
lion), together with contributions from the Government (US$8.7 million). 1/
The co-donors have reached agreement on terms assuring financing of the
realignment. Finalization of these agreements, or assurances that funding will
otherwise be forthcoming, will be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed
Loan.
1/   ADB - African Development Bank; BADEA - Arab Bank for Economic Develop-
ment in Africa; CCCE - Caisse Centrale de Cooperation Economique; CIDA
- Canadian International Development Agency; COFACE - Compagnie Francaise
de Credit pour l'Exportation; EIB - European Investment Bank; FAC -
Fonds d'Aide et de Cooperation; FED - Fonds Europeen de Developpement;
KDF - Kuwait Fund for Economic Development; SFD - Saudi Fund for
Development.



- iii -
viii.     Individual items in the investment plan outside the realignment
have been or will be financed by CCCE/COFACE (US$33.4 million), ATC (US$21.6
million), ADB (US$9.9 million), FED/EIB (US$4.7 million), FAC (US$4.6 million),
Bank (US$3.8 million), Congo banks (US$2.7 million), COMILOG (US$0.8 million)
and the Government (US$3.5 million). Although much of the financing of these
items has been secured, a condition of effectiveness of the proposed Loan
will be the receipt by the Bank of satisfactory assurances for financing not
yet secured.
ix.       The proposed Loan of US$38.0 million will consist of US$31.0 million
towards the CFCO realignment, US$3.8 million for ATC training facilities and
technical assistance, US$0.2 million for retroactive financing of railcar
bogies and radio equipment for the CFCO, and US$3.0 million for capitaliza-
tion of interest on the proposed Loan. The Government had originally requested
financing of the railcar bogies and radio equipment under Credit 297-COB
for the First Railway Project, but agreed to the Bank's proposal of retroactive
financing of these items under the proposed Loan.
x.        ATC will be responsible for execution of the project.  Consultants
Tecsult (Canada) who carried out the detailed engineering for the realign-
ment, have been retained to supervise the civil works for this item. Bids
from prequalified contractors for these works have been evaluated in accord-
ance with Bank guidelines. A?C intends to procure imported equipment and
materials for the realignment and training facilities by international compe-
titive bidding or alternative procedure acceptable to the Bank. Consultants
providing technical assistance will be selected on terms and conditions accept-
able to the Bank. The Bank will have the principal 'role in overall project
supervision.
xi.       ATC enjoys sufficient autonomy in its operations to permit effec-
tive management. The quality of top management is generally satisfactory
although it relies heavily on expatriate technical assistance. There is a
need, however, to increase the participation of local staff in decision-making,
and to provide training in view of progressing Africanization. Although ATC
provides good basic training for apprentices and recruits, some reorganization
and expansion of facilities is needed to make this training more effective.
The proposed project provides for the required additional facilities, and for
about 40 man-years of technical assistance for on-the-job training and for
training of instructors.
xii.      ATC's physical assets are reasonably well maintained.  At Pointe
Noire port, additional timber storage facilities and quays for handling
increased general cargo traffic and large manganese ore ships are being
extended. The Government has recently agreed with three private cargo-
handling firms to establish separate joint enterprises, one for timber
traffic which started operations in 1975, and two for general cargo due to



-iv-
commence operations during 1976. With these enterprises and the new facili-
ties, the port should be able to improve its operations and meet traffic
forecast for 1980. Efforts are being made to keep the CFCO track in good
repair, although maintenance costs on the Holle-Loubomo section are high due
to the age and low technical standards of the line, and the condition of the
structures. Completion of ongoing extensions to crossings and stations and
of signalling improvements will permit longer and heavier trains to operate
on this section starting in 1976, and enable the CFCO to meet traffic forecast
to 1979 when the realignment will be completed.  At Brazzaville port, extensions
are being carried out to the general cargo and log wharves, and the marine
workshops are to be enlarged. The river transport fleet is being expanded
and the river channels improved to allow longer annual navigable periods.
Operating targets to be achieved by 1978 are proposed for all sections of ATC.
xiii.     ATC's future transport role is expected to remain that of a regional
carrier for the Congo, CAR, southeastern Cameroon, southern Gabon, and to a
lesser extent, southern Chad. The principal freight traffic using the ATC
system will continue to be manganese from Gabon during the coming years, and
timber in the longer term. Next in importance are general cargo, bulk oil,
and CFCO passenger traffic. Traffic in manganese is expected to increase to
2.6 million ton p.a. by 1981, and to remain at this level until 1985; some
manganese might then be diverted to the new Transgabon railway that would
start operating in 1986, although the CFCO, once realigned, could handle the
2 million tons allocated to it under an inter-governmental agreement between
Gabon and Congo. Although it is assumed that the present world market slump
in timber will continue through 1976, Pointe Noire port is expected to handle
about 900,000 tons in 1978 and about 2.0 million tons in 1985, mainly as a
result of opening up forest reserves in northern Congo.
xiv.      The economic analysis of the ATC investment program focussed on the
CFCO realignment which is the most critical and costly element of the project.
The benefits from the realignment were assessed in terms of operating cost
savings and the avoidance of traffic frustration due to increased capacity.
The realignment was evaluated against various investment alternatives, in-
cluding a minimal rehabilitation program, a major improvement of the existing
line, and a combination of the minimal program and construction of a road be-
tween Brazzaville and Pointe Noire. Alternative cases were considered
regarding operating efficiency and assignment of traffic priorities. Under
the most probable case, the economic return of the realignment project com-
pared with the minimal program is estimated at 18%; higher returns are obtained
when the realignment is compared to the other investment alternatives. The
economic return for the whole ATC investment plan is estimated at 23%. These
returns would apply to the entire region served by the ATC, with about half
accruing to the Congo.
xv.       ATC's poor traffic performance and inadequate tariff policies over
1970-74 resulted in a deteriorating financial position, and short-term lia-
bilities exceeded current assets at the end of the period. Despite tariff
increases totalling about 50% during 1973/74, ATC's 1975 financial performance



v -
worsened because of reduced demand for timber traffic and unforeseen Government-
ordered wage increases. The financial situation is expected to remain diffi-
cult in 1976-79 mainly due to the anticipated slow recovery of the depressed
world timber market. In view of the above, the Government and ATC agreed to
take all measures necessary, including tariff increases and a stringent con-
trol of ATC's operating expenses, to achieve a minimum annual rate of return
on fixed assets of 6% in 1977,and 8% from 1978 through 1980. In addition,
the Government will alleviate ATC's financial situation by waiving taxes
(mainly sales taxes) amounting to CFAF 4 billion in 1975-80. Furthermore,
in order to ease ATC's debt service burden, the proposed Loan will include
US$3 million to capitalize interest during project implementation. As a
result of the above measures, ATC's operating sections should all be finan-
cially viable by 1980. However, to allow for a review of its annual finan-
cial performance, ATC will forward its draft annual budgets to the Bank for
comment prior to submitting them for approval by ATC's board, so that remedial
measures if required, can be undertaken promptly in consultation with the Bank.
An annual rate of return on net fixed assets of 8% during 1980-86 would
enable ATC to build up substantial cash revenues by the end of the period,
while fully restoring its working capital to planned levels, financing renewals
and investments without external assistance, and servicing its debts. A
sensitivity analysis was conducted under the assumptions that gross operating
revenues decrease by 20%, working expenses increase by 10%, and the cost of
capital investments, excluding the CFCO realignment, increase by 10%. Assuming
the combined effect of the above assumptions, ATC's financial position would
rPmain acceptable provided that tariffs are raised in line with projected
inflation levels, and that ATC can borrow up to about US$35 million in 1976-
79 and up to about 40% of its 1980-86 planned investments.
xvi.      The proposed project is suitable for a Bank Loan of US$38 million
to ATC with the guarantee of the Government of the People's Republic of the
Congo. The Loan term will be 25 years including five years of grace, at an
interest rate of 8-1/2%.






CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
1. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Agence Transcongolaise des Communications (ATC), formed in 1969,
is the-successor to a multinational agency, the Agence Transequatoriale des
Communications (ATEC), created in 1958 by the Congo, Gabon, Central African
Republic (CAR), and Chad to manage the 2,900 km route from N'Djamena (Chad),
through Bangui (CAR) to Pointe Noire (Congo) (Map 2254R7). Now a Congo national
agency, ATC still performs many functions of the former ATEC. ATC owns and
operates: (a) the Atlantic Ocean port of Pointe Noire; (b) a railway, Chemin
de Fer Congo-Ocean (CFCO) from Pointe Noire to Brazzaville; and (c) the river
ports and services (transport, navigation, maintenance of navigable channels)
on the Congo-Oubangui-Sangha rivers.
1.02      Traffic on the ATC system grew rapidly until 1972 when capacity
became insufficient to meet the demand. ATC is carrying out improvements on
all its sections, but the existing CFCO alignment winding through a mountain
range presents a major restriction. Realignment of about 106 km was proposed
in a feasibility study financed by the United Nations Development Programme
(UNDP) and carried out in 1971/72 by consultants Tecsult (Canada); the alterna-
tive of staged improvement of the existing alignment was found to be imprac-
tical. The Bank Group consequently financed final engineering of the realign-
ment proposed by Tecsult under its First Railway Project (Credit 297-COB,
US$6.3 million, April 1972, increased to US$6.9 million in July 1973), which
also provided for a traffic forecasting and costing study for ATC carried out
by consultants BCEOM/Sofrerail (France), and for the purchase of freight-cars
for general traffic. The project has been implemented reasonably well,
although its expected completion date is about two years later than originally
planned, due to delays in financing, finalizing contracts, and execution of
non-IDA financed items; a new Closing Date of March 31, 1976 has been agreed
with the Government.
1.03      ATC has prepared a five-year investment plan for 1974-78 which is
expected to cost approximately US$233.8 million equivalent (net of taxes),
including physical and price contingencies. The proposed project covers this
investment plan which includes the following principal items:
(i)  ATC Headquarters:  improvement of training facilities and
programs (US$5.5 million);
(ii)  Pointe Noire Port:  provision of an additional stacking area
for timber-handling, construction of new berths for manganese
and general cargo, and dredging (US$12.8 million);
(iii)  River Services:  procurement of mechanical handling equipment,
purchase of additional floating craft, expansion of Brazzaville
workshops, and deepening of river channels (US$21.1 million);
and



- 2 -
(iv)  CFCO Railway:  procurement and renewal of locomotives and rolling
stock, and general investments including track renewal, installation
of a signalling system, and expansion of the main equipment workshops
(US$45.6 million); and realignment of 88 km between Fourastie and
Loubomo (ex-Dolisie) and track improvements for the 18 km between
Holle and Fourastie (US$148.8 million).
ATC has been able to arrange financing to cover the items in the investment
plan (paras 4.10-4.12). The CFCO realignment, which is the largest compo-
nent of the plan, will be financed by a consortium in the following amounts
(converted into US$ equivalent at present exchange rates):

1.04      In addition to the First Railway Project, Bank Group lending for
transportation in the Congo has consisted of three Credits totalling US$6.1
million for highway development. The first project (Credit 56-COB, US$0.63
million, May 1969) for engineering and feasibility studies was completed in
June 1971. The second project (Credit 189-COB, US$1.5 million, May 1970) for
improvement of the Pointe Noire-Makola Road (34 km) was completed in October
1973. Although the third project (Credit 274-COB, US$4.0 million, December
1971) providing for reorganization and improvement of highway maintenance
had to be scaled down due to cost increases and lack of Government funds, it
was completed on schedule at end-1975.



- 3 -
1.05      The appraisal of the proposed Second Railway Project is based on
information supplied by the Government, ATC, and consultants Tecsult and
BCEOM/Sofrerail, and on the findings of a Bank mission in November 1974
comprising Messrs. D. Johnson (Engineer), E. Chittleburgh (Technical Educator),
H. Georg (Engineer), J. Kesson (Engineer - Consultant), P. Levy (Financial
Analyst), T. Nkodo (Economist), and A. Weckerle (Economist).
2. THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
A.   Economic Setting
2.01      The People's Republic of the Congo covers an area of about 342,000
km2, with a total population estimated in 1974 at about 1.3 million. About
40% of the population live in urban agglomerations located mostly along the
Pointe Noire-Brazzaville railroad and in the economically active southern
regions of the country.
2.02      Until recently, Congo's development potential was based mainly on
the exploitation of its extensive forest reserves which cover about 60% of the
territory.  The resource base has since been broadened with the expansion of
crude oil production to about 2.4 million m tons in 1974, together with the
boost in oil prices.  Preliminary estimates indicate that per capita Gross
National Product (GNP) in 1974 was about US$385 at average 1972-74 prices.
In 1975, however, oil production fell by 25 percent, the non-oil sector of the
economy stagnated in large part owing to the drop in international demand for
raw materials, and import prices and public expenditures suffered the full
impact of inflation. Since Government expenditure plans had assumed increased
revenues from oil and other sources, the abrupt reversal left little time to
stabilize a deteriorating public finance situation. In view of this negative
trend and the current expectation of more modest growth prospects for oil and
for development generally, the new Government which came to power at end-1975
has undertaken an austerity program to restrain expenditure and to improve
financial management. The proposed project will contribute greatly to the
country's real growth and to increased export earnings by ensuring the trans-
port capacity necessary for development of the rich forest reserves in the
Sangha and Oubangui river basins which are now almost unexploited. Further,
the additional labor income that would be generated by the establishment and
expansion of logging and timber processing operatlos in these regions could
become an important factor in slowing down the present level of migration to
urban centers.
B.   The Transport System
2.03       Congo's situation on the Atlantic coast with a favorably located
seaport and the existence of navigable rivers has resulted in development
of an extensive transport infrastructure to serve Congo's own trade flows
as well as transit traffic for neighboring landlocked Chad, CAR, and the
southern regions of Cameroon and Gabon. This transit traffic accounted in
1975 for about 65% of total freight movements on the CFCO railway; the transit



-4-
share is however expected to decline over the long term to less than 50%,
mainly due to Congo's own traffic, in particular timber, expanding faster
than that of the other countries served by ATC.
2.04      The transport system is made up of two separate networks.  The
backbone of the infrastructure is the Transequatorial Route comprising the
CFCO railway line (510 km) between the two major ports at Pointe Noire and
Brazzaville, and the navigable sections of the Congo and Oubangui rivers
(about 1,120 km). The other major links in this primary transport network
are a 285 km railway owned and operated by COMILOG (Gabon) for the transport
of manganese ore, the Loubomo-Gabon road (about 250 km), and the Sangha river
which is navigable over about 480 km.
2.05      Congo also has a large road network totalling about 10,800 km, but
only about 3,000 km (including 300 km of paved roads) provide all-weather
service. The major road axis connects Brazzaville and Ouesso in the north
(about 900 km); the rest of the system consists of secondary and forestry
roads located mostly in the area west of Brazzaville, which contains about
two-thirds of the population and the important centers of economic activity.
Only a small proportion of freight traffic is carried by road, and about 70%
of this is timber traffic in the south. The Bank is planning to appraise a
further highway project in June 1976 which will include road maintenance as
well as engineering studies of the Zanaga-Sibiti-Loudima road (about 225 km).
When completed, this road will help assure the evacuation of timber from the
Zabaga region to the railhead at Loudima.
2.06      There are eight airports and about thirty minor airfields.  Only
three (Brazzaville, Pointe Noire, and Loubomo) have paved runways, and only
Brazzaville is equipped to handle long-range jet aircraft serving interna-
tional traffic. A Government-owned company, Lina Congo, provides scheduled
domestic services.
C.   Transport Policy and Planning
2.07      The Ministry of Public Works and Transport (MPWT) is responsible
for devising and enforcing transport policy. Transport coordination and
planning outside the ATC has not been an issue in the past because of the
dominant role of the ATC system which carries about 90% of land transport
traffic. However, the Government's planned development and upgrading of
the Pointe Noire-Brazzaville-Ouesso road axis which runs parallel to the
ATC system now makes the planning and coordination of transport services an
important issue. A geographically large country such as the Congo with a
considerable resource potential requires a transport system which is sufficient-
ly versatile to permit the development of this potential, and long-range plan-
ning geared to developing a network that can adequately meet this need. How-
ever, there would still remain substantial questions of matching transport
investments to actual requirements in the axis, and of establishing the
pricing policies necessary to ensure a proper role for road and rail services.
The administrative machinery necessary to carry out these tasks will be pro-
vided by the planning unit recently established in MPWT following the
Government's instruction during 1975 that such units be set up in all



-5-
Ministries. MPWT has already designated a Director for its unit for which
terms of reference are being discussed with the Bank. The main tasks of the
unit will be to: (a) analyze the costs and benefits of proposed investments
in the various modes to achieve an optimum distribution and timing of
these investments; (b) review and keep up-to-date the transport pricing
policy; and (c) ensure that investment proposals for transport take into con-
sideration the requirements of other sectors of the economy. Further, as the
proposed project constitutes the major investment in the transport sector
for the near future, the Government has agreed to consult with the Bank prior
to undertaking any major investments in the sector outside the ATC investment
program.
3. AGENCE TRANSCONGOLAISE DES COMMUNICATIONS (ATC)
A.   Organization, Management, and Staffing
3.01      Administration of ATC is by a Board with the Minister of Public
Works and Transport as Chairman, and 13 members consisting of other Ministers
and representatives from the staff and the ruling political party. The
Board can make all decisions or delegate its powers to the Chairman except
in the case of: (i) tariffs, budgets, and investment programs, which require
the approval of the Minister of Public Works and Transport; and (ii) loan
conditions, which must be approved by the Council of Ministers (see Organiza-
tion Chart). The Board operates in a satisfactory manner.
3.02      ATC has a General Manager and an Assistant General Manager who are
appointed by the Council of State on the recommendation of the Board. A
Financial Controller is nominated by the Minister of Finance. The General
Manager has adequate freedom to deal with ATC's normal business. His head-
quarters section provides budget and investment planning and administration
for the three operational sections (ports, river services, and CFCO), each
of which is self-contained operationally and financially under the direction
of separate managers. Officials and staff of ATC are mostly Congolese assisted
by 25 foreign contract employees and about 70 technical experts provided by
France.
3.03      The management and development planning of ATC and its sections is
competent, but relies considerably on the expatriate technical assistants,
some of whom fill key management positions.  Operations of the sections
(particularly the CFCO) have also been largely guided by technical assistants,
but in mid-1972 many were replaced by Congolese, and the role of technical
assistance has since then been essentially confined to training (Table 1). At
the same time, the number of Congolese permanent staff has been increasing
steadily, and at end-1974 totalled over 6,000, representing more than a 25%
increase above the 1970 level (Table 2). Partly as a result of all the above,
the overall operating performance of ATC, and particularly of the CFCO, has
been declining. To deal with this situation, proposals are made under the
present project for improved training, increased technical assistance, and



- 6 -
the achievement of specified operational targets (paras. 3.04-3.06). To
ensure the effectiveness of the additional technical assistance, the Govern-
ment has provided assurances that the present level of such assistance
available to ATC and the responsibilities of technical assistants in line
positions will not be reduced without prior approval of the Bank.
3.04      ATC provides good basic training for apprentices in the mechanical
equipment workshops and for new recruits in other departments, but some re-
organization and expansion of facilities is needed to make the training more
effective. In addition, in-service training for upgrading the skills of
existing staff is inadequate and poorly organized. Under the proposed pro-
ject,the direction of all training will be centralized under a personnel
officer attached to the General Manager's staff, who will also be responsible
for recruitment, personnel records, and staff development for all sections
of ATC. In addition, the personnel officer will be responsible for the pre-
paration of a manpower plan for ATC, to be completed by 1977.
3.05      In association with the training schemes, increased technical
assistance will be provided for on-the-job training in selected areas includ-
ing workshop organization, track and building maintenance, traffic and com-
mercial operations, communications and signalling, accounting, and training
of instructors for the schools and for the development of middle-management
personnel.
3.06      Training and technical assistance requirements are reviewed in
detail in Annex 1; draft terms of reference and conditions for all the pro-
posed services, as well as arrangements for their implementation, have been
discussed and agreed with ATC (para. 4.03).
B.   Budgeting, Accounting, Auditing, and Insurance
3.07      ATC was formed in 1969, and its controlling statute (Decree No.
70/738 of February 11, 1970) provides for the agency to have financial
autonomy and to operate on a commercial basis. The three ATC operating
sections have individual budgets and investment plans, and have to cover
expenses through their own revenues. However, the statute provides for
financial solidarity of all the ATC sections, specifying that surpluses in
one section would be used to cover deficits in another. The arrangements
appear reasonable in view of the interdependence of the whole ATC transport
system.
3.08      Accounting is done on a commercial basis under the direct responsi-
bility of the Chief Accountant and the overall supervision of the Financial
Controller. The accounting system is generally satisfactory. Assets have
been revalued, and the method of entering depreciation provisions and finan-
cial charges modified, as agreed with the Bank under the First Railway Project.
A new accounting plan ("Plan comptable OCAM" 1/) is now being introduced in
1/ Organisation Commune Africaine et Malgache.



francophone West Africa, including the Congo. As ATC's accounting system
needs some improvement, consulting services are included in the proposed
project to: (a) adopt the OCAM accounting plan to ATC's requirements;
(b) improve financial and statistical information required by management;
(c) set up a comprehensive costing system; and (d) establish a cost-related
tariffing system.
3.09      Following agreement reached under Credit 297-COB, a firm of inde-
pendent auditors acceptable to the Bank Group was appointed to audit ATC's
accounts. As their report dealing with the 1974 accounts complied with the
Bank Group's auditing requirements, the same firm will now continue to audit
the accounts for 1975 onwards. Accordingly, the Government and ATC have
agreed that this independent audit will continue, and that the audited
accounts will be submitted to the Bank not later than six months after the
end of each fiscal year.
3.10      ATC has insurance coverage for:  road vehicles (as legally required);
equipment or buildings rented or hired out (and for which the hirer or tenant
pays); staff, against accidents at work; harbor floating craft; river floating
craft; river port buildings (including equipment) against fire; river port
goods while in ATC storage, against fire; and goods on river craft under ATC
responsibility, against third party risks. All other normal risks are met
from ATC's own funds, which is an acceptable practice.
C. Property
3.11      ATC Headquarters, as well as the railway and the seaport administra-
tion, are located at Pointe Noire. The facilities at the port of Pointe Noire
comprise five berths for general cargo and two for timber, as well as spe-
cialized berths for bulk oil, manganese ore, and fishing vessels. Additional
storage facilities for timber have recently been completed, and quays for
handling increased volumes of general cargo and larger manganese ore ships
are being extended. The CFCO railway is reasonably well-equipped with loco-
motives and rolling stock, but large sections of the track and infrastructure
are inadequate for present and forecast volumes of freight traffic (see para.
3.12). The principal river transport route to Bangui (CAR) is about 1,120 km
long, and the subsidiary river network serving northeast Congo and southeast
Cameroon totals about 3,800 km. The river navigation and clearance of most
of the waterway channels are provided by the Service Commun des Voies Navigables,
an ATC/ACCF 1/ enterprise which works satisfactorily. At Brazzaville, there
are wharves for general cargo and timber traffic, and also facilities for bulk
oil and passenger traffic. A new wharf area for handling timber logs has re-
cently been completed. Extensions are being carried out to the general cargo
and log wharves, and the marine workshops are to be enlarged. The river
transport fleets of ATC and ACCF are being expanded, and the river channels
are gradually being improved. Property details for each section of ATC are
given in Annexes 2 and 3.
1/   Agence Centrafricaine des Communications Fluviales, an agency of the
Government of the Central African Republic.



-8 -
CFCO Railway
3.12      The CFCO line was built at minimum cost between 1921 and 1934 with
an infrastructure and track standards designed for light traffic. The line
has a narrow roadbed, steep gradients and sharp curves, particularly over two
mountain ranges, one near the coast and the other towards Brazzaville. Traffic
has increased considerably over the past 20 years, particularly over the 200 km
between the junction of the COMILOG railway at Mont Bello and Pointe Noire,
and further traffic increases are foreseen (Table 3). This increased traffic
is much beyond that envisaged when the line was constructed, and carried on
long trains and heavy axle loads, has led to failure of the track structure,
high rate of rail wear, breakage of welded joints, and collapse of the
superstructure of the numerous concrete arch viaducts. These failures in
turn cause major maintenance problems with the track and equipment, frequent
accidents, considerable train delays, and consequent restrictions on the
traffic carried. The general condition of the viaducts is also poor, while
the Bamba tunnel (1.7 km) is in dangerous condition and needs rehabilitation.
The facilities for controlling train movements and for crossing trains at
stations have also been poor, and while ATC has made some improvements com-
pleted at end-1974, the possibilities for future development are restricted
by physical limitations. The existing alignment and track from Pointe Noire
to Holle were improved to an adequate standard in 1968-70, but the Bank con-
cluded in 1968 that very substantial betterment of the existing conditions
would be necessary for the mountainous Holle-Loubomo section. A subsequent
feasibility study recommended construction of about 106 km of completely new
route (Map 11276 R). While the recommendation has been modified to include
88 km of new construction and about 18 km of line improvement, this invest-
ment represents a large part of the ATC investment plan, since immediate
action is needed to avoid major line interruptions threatened by failure of
the track and structures over the Holle-Loubomo section. Needed improve-
ments on the remaining section between Loubomo and Brazzaville are not so
extensive or critical, and can follow in subsequent investment plans.
D.   Operations
3.13      Details of operations of each section of ATC are given in Annex 4,
and summaries of operational statistics are in Tables 4-6. The various
operating targets for Pointe Noire Port, CFCO, and river transport services,
discussed in the following paragraphs and detailed in Annex 5, have been
agreed with ATC.
Pointe Noire Port
3.14      Ship movements are the responsibility of the port organization, but
all cargo-handling (on ship and on shore) is done by three private firms which
provide their own equipment. The plant and labor normally operate on a single
shift, with an occasional second shift. These arrangements have allowed a
reasonable throughput of cargo, but increased volumes would need more equip-
ment and a regular second shift operation, as required. The Government and
ATC have recently agreed with the cargo-handling companies to establish three
joint enterprises. The first and largest of these, dealing with all timber
traffic, started operations in 1975; the two others for general cargo are



-9-
scheduled to commence operations in 1976. The new enterprises will be
expected to obtain essential equipment with CCCE financing. It is intended
that they will also: (i) operate regular second shifts as and when required;
(ii) remove long-term timber log storage from the port to areas near the CFCO
marshalling yard at Pointe Noire; and (iii) provide improved statistical data
on cargo operations. With these improvements, as well as the facilities under
constructon and increased output targets, it should be possible for the port
to meet forecast traffic requirements until about 1980 (Table 7). The Govern-
ment has provided assurances that it will ensure that the three joint enter-
prises make required investments and take other measures as necessary to
assure the orderly flow of traffic at Pointe Noire.
CFCO Railway
3.15      The principal traffic carried is COMILOG manganese ore, potash from
the Compagnie des Potasses du Congo (CPC), timber, and general cargo. COMILOG
provides its own locomotives, rolling stock and train staff for operations over
the sections Mbinda-Mont Bello-Pointe Noire. Three train paths each way per
day are at COMILOG's disposal. The operating arrangements for COMILOG trains
and for CFCO trains on the COMILOG railway are covered by agreements between
ATC and COMILOG. Potash is carried 47 km from Makola to CPC's own sea-loading
installation at Pointe Noire. CPC provides the bulk freight cars, while CFCO
supplies the motive power and operates the trains. Although the traffic now
only requires one train each way per day, capacity exists for up to three
trains per day (possibly required by 1985). Timber traffic reaches the CFCO
railway mainly at Mont Bello and Brazzaville.
3.16      The increasing timber and general traffic has been carried partly
by expanding the fleet of locomotives and cars, and partly by increasing the
size of individual units. However, because track and infrastructure problems
between Holle and Loubomo limit the number of trains possible, CFCO traffic
has remained constant since 1971 (Table 3). On completion of the extensions
of crossings and stations and the signalling improvements now underway, longer
trains will be able to operate starting in 1976. The capacity increases thus
provided will meet forecast needs only to 1979, however, and will not provide
a long-term solution to the problems of increasing capacity or improving pre-
sent difficult operating conditions. Even on completion of the proposed new
Holle-Loubomo section (which will improve operating and maintenance conditions
over approximately 106 km out of the total line of 510 km), actual railway
capacity will continue to depend on higher staff efficiency to be developed
under the expanded training and technical assistance proposed under the pro-
posed project. To improve CFCO operations, targets for achievement by 1978
have been agreed (Annex 5).
3.17      Equipment availability is poor (averaging 57% for locomotives and
64% for railcars in 1974) due to unsatisfactory operating conditions, poor
maintenance, and the acquisition of some locomotives which were initially
unsuitable for local conditions. As ATC is expanding the main workshop facil-
ities and intends also to reorganize its maintenance operations, it should be
possible to increase average locomotive availability by 1978 to about 76%,
which is an agreed operational target.



- 10 -
River Transport
3.18      ATC is responsible for the transshipment at Brazzaville of all cargo
between river and railway. The division between the ATC and ACCF river fleets
of the freight volume, as well as the nature of the cargo, is based on an
agreement between the Governments of Congo and CAR. The fleets are being
increased to meet requirements in accordance with this agreement (Table 8).
ATC operations in river transport are reasonably efficient, but depend on
the vagaries of river flows and levels. Programs for improving river navi-
gation have been in progress for several years, and operating conditions
are gradually improving on the upper reaches of the rivers. Due to some dec-
line in overall river port and transport operations since 1971, targets to
be achieved by 1978 have been agreed under the proposed project.
E.  ATC Investment Program
3.19      ATC prepared a five-year investment plan for the period 1971-75 as
part of the First Railway Project. This plan has been reviewed and updated
periodically by ATC, and most recently in association with BCEOM/Sofrerail.
The resulting plan for 1974-78, with some agreed modifications, is set out in
Tables 9a and b, with details of the proposed financing in Annex 8. The
total cost for the plan including physical and price contingencies is about
CFAF 52.6 billion net of taxes, comprising CFAF 43.74 billion for CFCO, CFAF
4.74 billion for river ports and services, CFAF 2.89 billion for Pointe Noire
port, and CFAF 1.23 billion for miscellaneous services. ATC has agreed that
it will make no change in the investment plan for 1974-78 (eccept for minor
items totalling not more than CFAF 200 million annually) unless in agreement
with the Bank.
3.20      ATC and BCEOM/Sofrerail have also assessed the investment program
needed for the period 1979-85; the proposed program totals, at 1974 prices,
CFAF 35.0 billion. (Table 10). ATC has agreed to review this program with
the Bank by December 31, 1977 to ascertain any modifications required by
conditions pertaining at that time. An assessment of traffic capacities on
each section of ATC required according to the traffic forecasts, and to be
provided according to the investment program, is shown in Table 11.

4.02      The 1974 and 1975 items in the investment plan amounting to about
US$18.4 million equivalent have been or are being implemented. Estimates
for the Pointe Noire port, river ports and services, and general items for
the CFCO railway, are based on 1974 costs, existing contracts, and recent
quotations. The estimate for the realignment (in 1975 prices) is based on
bids received for the main civil works contract (about 80% of the total
estimate for the realignment) and on recent contracts and quotations. Physi-
cal contingencies for the various sections, excluding the realignment, have
been provided at 10% on the costs of works, and about 5% on the costs of
equipment. For the realignment, physical contingencies of 15% have been
provided on local and foreign costs of the tunnelling in view of the uncertain-
ties involved in such work, 5% on the local and foreign costs of structures,
3.5% on the foreign costs of rails and fastenings, and 10% on local and
foreign costs for the other items. Price contingencies have been calculated
at 16% on civil works and 12% on equipment for 1975, and at decreasing rates
thereafter. Overall, the price contingency element allows for cost increases
for the project averaging 22% on sections excluding the realignment, and 28%
on the realignment. The Government has agreed that all materials and equip-
ment used for the realignment will be exempt from all taxes and duties;
further, the contractor selected for the main civil works contract will be
exempted from local turnover taxes. 1/
ATC Headquarters
4.03      Needed improvements to the training methods and facilities for all
sections of ATC will be met by the provision of additional classrooms and
equipment, and some reorganization of the operations and training programs.
Technical assistance services will be required to make recommendations and
carry out the programs in training and in operations, particularly on the
CFCO railway. The proposed project provides for a total of 40 man-years of
technical assistance services by about 17 experts (see Annex 1).
Pointe Noire Port
4.04      The proposed investments are mainly to complete ongoing work in-
cluding additional timber log-handling facilities, new berths for manganese
ore and general cargo, improved elipway facilities, and dredging. No other
major Investment should be required before 1978 if output and staff performance
are improved as expected, and if the planned use of additional equipment and
of a regular second shift is realized.
River Ports and Services
4.05      To meet future traffic requirements, the river ports require some
minor improvements or extensions, additional mechanical handling equipment,
and renewal of existing equipment. The proposed project provides for all of
the above, and also for expansion of the main workshops at Brazzaville to
1/   Decree No. 75/17 of January 7, 1975, Decree No. 75/82 of February 24,
1975 and Decree No. 75/211 of May 28, 1975.



- 13 -
handle the increasing river fleet. Proposed expenditure on river navigation
is to continue the deepening of river channels and improvement of navigational
aids to allow longer annual navigable periods.
4.06      ATC and ACCF are planning to purchase additional river transport
units in accordance with their agreed traffic allocation. The purchase of
tugs and barges in 1974/75 will suffice for traffic forecast to 1978 (Table 8).
ATC will however require further units for use in 1978-80, and in view of the
long delivery periods of up to two years plus local assembly time, the pro-
posed project includes financing for additional tugs and lighters in 1977.
CFCO Railway
4.07      The general investments include:  (i) continuation of required
track renewal, mainly on the section Holle-Loubomo until the new line is
in service; (ii) completion of work on lengthening crossing loops and in-
stallation of signalling at selected stations to allow operation of longer
and heavier trains; (iii) improvement of marshalling yard facilities at
Pointe Noire and Brazzaville to allow operation of larger freight trains;
and (iv) completion of extensions to mechanical equipment workshops at Pointe
Noire to assure improved maintenance. Additional investments are planned
for purchase of motive power and rolling stock as follows: 10 main-line
diesel locomotives (5 as additions and 5 as replacements); and 10 passenger
cars (some as replacements, and some to handle increasing traffic). In the
case of the locomotives, allowance has been made for the foreseen improvement
in availability.
4.08      The investment plan proposes realignment of the Holle-Loubomo sec-
tion comprising improvements adjacent to the existing route over 18 km, and
complete re-routing of 88 km. Compared with the existing section, the new
alignment will have better grades and curvature, a wider roadbed, improved
drainage, and stronger bridges, tunnels, and other structures. Construction
will involve a considerable amount of earthworks, tunnelling and bridging,
as the terrain is very rugged with steep valleys. After the new route is
completed, ATC will retain the existing railway line for about a year as an
operational safeguard. The realignment will result in significantly reduced
operating costs, and will also allow an increase in freight traffic capacity
on the Pointe Noire-Holle-Loubomo section to cover the requirements forecast
for 1995 and beyond. The basic technical characteristics for realignment and
improvement of the railway are shown in Annex 7.
B.   Execution
4.09      ATC will be responsible for execution of the project.  It has
employed consultants to assist in the assessment and design of certain pro-
ject items, in particular the CFCO realignment. ATC will continue to employ
Tecsult under CIDA financing to supervise the civil works of the realignment,
and other consultants as needed for the project. In order to assure efficient
planning and control of the realignment works, ATC has formed under its
General Manager a small unit comprising seven technical assistants and one



- 14 -
Congolese engineer to handle coordination and contact with co-donors, payment
of construction certificates, and ATC's ordering and delivery of items such
as track and signalling material. The Government, ATC, and the co-donors
have no objection to the Bank supervising both those project items in whose
financing it is participating, and also all other items, to ensure the coordina-
tion and quality of the whole investment plan.
C.   Financing and Procurement
4.10      Details of the financing of ATC's 1974-78 Investment Plan are given
in Annex 8 and summarized in the following paragraphs. The co-donors have
reached agreement on terms assuring financing of the realignment. Finalization
of these agreements, or assurances satisfactory to the Bank that funding will
otherwise be forthcoming, will be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed
Loan. Much of the financing of the investment plan, excluding the realignment,
has already been secured; an additional condition of effectiveness of the
proposed Loan will be the receipt of assurances satisfactory to the Bank from
donors whose contributions have been agreed upon in principle but not yet
secured.
4.11      The realignment works have been divided into several components
partly to meet the requirements of some donors who insisted on parallel financ-
ing.  The breakdown of the realignment and its financing is summarized
on the following page:

The Bank's contribution of US$3.8 million (CFAF 0.85 billion equivalent) will

ATC given in Annex 1. Building extensions for the training schools estimated
at about US$400,000 equivalent will be let by ATC as local contracts following
local bidding procedures acceptable to the Bank.  Imported materials required
and equipment to be installed will be obtained by international competitive
bidding, except for small items purchased under local procedures satisfactory
to the Bank. The consultants providing technical assitance will be selected
on terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank. All other items in the in-
vestment plan, excluding the realignment, will be procured in accordance with
the individual procedures of the donors involved.
4.13      An amount of US$200,000 is included in the proposed Loan to cover
the foreign exchange costs of new bogies for CFCO railcars and for radio
equipment. The railcars purchased in 1965 are of light construction, and
replacement of their bogies will extend their useful service life. The radio
equipment will be used for the control of shunting movements in the marshalling
yards at Pointe Noire and Brazzaville, and to help the CFCO attain operational
improvements foreseen under the proposed project. The Government had requested
that funds remaining under the First Railway Project (Credit 297-COB) be used
for procurement of these two items; however, as the Bank had decided that only
items originally included in that first project could be financed from the
Credit, the Government agreed with the Bank's proposal that the bogies and
radio equipment be financed retroactively under the next Loan now proposed.
These items have been ordered under procedures satisfactory to the Bank, and
delivery has started.

D.   Disbursements
4.14      The total Loan under the proposed Second Railway Project would
be in the amount of US$38.0 million, comprising US$31.0 million towards the
CFCO realignment, US$3.8 million for training and technical assistance,
US$0.2 million for retroactive financing of railcar bogies and radio equip-
ment, and US$3.0 million towards capitalization of interest on the proposed
Loan.
4.15      Disbursements from the Loan Account will be made directly to ATC
for the foreign exchange costs of training facilities and for technical
assistance. Concerning the CFCO realignment, the Bank will participate
with the Government, FED, FAC, and CCCE in the joint financing of the
earthworks, structures and rail fastenings, with disbursements proportionate
to individual contributions. The schedule of estimated disbursements from
the Loan Account is given in Table 14. As the proposed Loan is to finance
specific objectives, any savings in the Loan Account on completion of the
project will be cancelled.
5. ECONOMIC EVALUATION
A.   Investment Objectives
5.01      The ATC system was developed several decades ago to meet the simple
tranaport requirements of the slowly emerging economies of Central Africa.
The growth of traffic demand resulting from a quickened pace of development
has now heavily taxed ATC's capacity and brought about a serious deterioration
of some of its physical assets. The proposed project, ATC's 1974-78 Invest-
ment Plan, and the long-range program to 1985, have therefore been devised
to safeguard the continued functioning of the transport route, improve oper-
ations, and provide increased capacity. The main and initial focus of the
planned investments is on the CFCO railway where immediate action is required
to avoid major line interruptions threatened by failure of the main tunnel
or viaducts, or by landslides.
B.   Traffic Projections and Assignment
5.02      ATC's present transport role is that of a carrier for the Congo,
CAR, southeastern Cameroon, southern Gabon, and to a lesser extent, also for
southern Chad. The principal freight traffic carried is timber from Congo
and neighboring countries, together with manganese from the COMILOG mining
operation in Gabon transported over 200 km of the CFCO railway to the port
of Pointe Noire. Next in importance are general cargo and bulk oil, mostly
from or to Congo and CAR. In addition, ATC operates the main line for
railway passenger traffic in Congo.



- 18 -
5.03      The traffic projections discussed below are subject to underlying
uncertainties concerning: (a) the timing of the recovery from the present
slump in the world timber market; (b) the rate of future timber exploitation
in Congo; (c) the effect of alternative evacuation routes being constructed
in Gabon (in particular, the Transgabonese railway) and considered for
Cameroon and CAR; and (d) the assumption that no major investments or sub-
stantial improvements will be made on the road from Brazzaville to Pointe
Noire at least within the next decade. The projections used in the analysis
represent traffic estimates based on information presently available. Table
3 shows the best traffic estimates by commodity for ATC, and Table 15 the
distribution of this traffic between Brazzaville and Pointe Noire by country
of origin and destination. The impact of potential changes in critical
traffic assumptions is incorporated in the economic evaluation through a
sensitivity analysis (para. 5.12).
5.04      The timber traffic projections detailed in Annex 9 are based on an
evaluation of future world timber markets taking into account growth of local
processing, a timber resource analysis of the Central African region poten-
tially to be evacuated by ATC, and an assessment of existing and future alter-
native evacuation routes. The projections assume that the present slump in
the world timber market will end in 1977, with timber traffic fully recovering
in 1978. Although no firm plans are in hand, it is conceivable that by 1980
or thereabouts, Cameroon and CAR could develop a transport route to provide
access to a Cameroon port for their forestry areas which presently depend on
ATC. Even then, however, ATC should continue to play its regional role on
the grounds of comparative transport cost advantages. Timber traffic pro-
jections over the CFCO railway assume that timber originating in southern
Gabon would, by 1985, be transferred to the Transgabon railway. A discussion
of alternatives to ATC timber evacuation routes is in Annex 10. Concerning
the exploitation of Congo's timber resources, the Government has agreed that
it will take all necessary measures (a) to ensure that concessions of
operating rights be granted in certain forestry areas in agreement with the
Bank, and (b) to promptly implement its policies relating to the inventory
and exploitation of other areas.
5.05      Traffic in manganese ore, presently about 2.3 million tons p.a., is
expected to increase to about 2.6 million tons p.a. by 1976, and then level
off until about 1985. The ceiling is determined, inter alia, by the capacity
of the cableway system linking the mine at Moanda (Gabon) with the railhead
at Mbinda (Congo), from where the ('OMILOG railway feeds the ore into the ATC
system. From 1985 on, it has been assumed for evaluation purposes only that
all the manganese would be carried by the Tranagabonese railway. The CFCO
railway would have available capacity to handle the manganese after 1985,
however, if required under the 1974 agreement between Congo and Gabon which
obligates the CFCO to carry a minimum of 2 million tons of manganese beyond
the year 2000.
5.06      Potash exports are forecast to increase from the current 400,000
tons to about 1.2 million tons by 1985. This tentative forecast reflects



- 19 -
the expectation that carmelite (a complex potassium compound) will be mined
when the existing potash deposits run out. Due to the short distance (about
43 ki) this traffic is carried by the CFCO to Pointe Noire, uncertainty about
the increased future volumes is not significant for the project.
5.07      No alternative Congo transport system exists as a substitute for
ATC's river services, railway, and ports. Pointe Noire is not yet linked to
Brazzaville by road (construction of only about 200 km of the 550 km distance
between these points is completed or planned), nor Brazzaville with areas to
the north beyond Ouesso. However, as the justification for the project end
the future viability of the CFCO depend in large measure on the CFCO carrying
all forecast traffic between Brazzaville and Pointe Noire, any major road
investment in this corridor could constitute an over-investment and create
transport coordination problems. Under the First Railway Project, the Gov-
ernment agreed that any major road construction between the two cities would
be subject to feasibility studies to be reviewed by the Association. The
Government has now agreed to consult with the Bank before undertaking any
major investments in the transport sector outside the ATC investment program
(para. 2.07).
C. Investment Alternatives
5.08      While a complete investment package for all ATC sections is required
to meet the objectives mentioned above, the choice of economic/technical
options to the proposed investments are essentially limited to the Pointe
Noire-Brazzaville railway, and particularly the proposed Fourastie-Louboio
realignment. Potential infrastructure solutions considered as alternatives
to the proposed realignment were: (i) realignment of the Rolle-Loubomo
section on a northern route; (ii) rehabilitation and improvement of the
existing Fourastie-Loubomo track; and (iii) complementing CFCO railway
capacity by a road.
5.09      The rail realignment on an alternative northern route was rejected,
as the capital and operating costs would be higher than for the southern
solution proposed under the project, while only yielding the same benefits.
Major improvements to the existing line would be inferior in terms of capacity,
operating characteristics, and economics as demonstrated in Annex 11, Part I.
Complete substitution of the CFCO railway by a road is also not an attractive
alternative, because for the traffic volumes and distances involved, the
resulting increases in transport costs would be far higher than the apparent
savings in capital expenditures. Partial substitution in the form of combining
a road with the existing railway (adequately maintained, but not improved)
would offer no better solution than the major improvements case when compared
to the proposed realignment.
D.  Economic Returns
5.10      Table 13 shows the economic rates of return of the realignment
compared with the investment alternatives given below, and of the total ATC
investments during 1974-78, together with the results of the sensitivity
analysis. The proposed realignment was evaluated against:



- 20 -
(a)  a reference case consisting of the minimum investment that
could be made to avoid further deterioration of the
Fourastie-Loubomo section of the railway. The rate of
return for the realignment is estimated at 18%;
(b)  a major improvement of the Fourastie-Dolisie section,
yielding a rate of return for the realignment of 20%; and
(c)  a combination of the reference case and a complementary road,
for alternative openings of the realignment and the road
in 1979 and 1983, yielding a rate of return of 22% in the first
case, and 15% in the second case.
The major benefit of the realignment in cases (a) and (b) above is the avoidance
of frustrated traffic of the Congo, Cameroon, and landlocked CAR and Chad.
Benefits from frustrated traffic are therefore estimated as the value added
foregone by the regional economy of these four countries. On a purely national
basis for the Congo, this project would have a lower but still acceptable rate
of return, as it is to be financed on concessionary terms by grants and soft
loans that will be available because of the regional importance of the proj-
ect, but which would not be available for other projects in the CoUgo.
5.11      The project and its implementation will, in addition, because of
its size in relation to the country's economy, have a significant effect on
the overall rate of growth of Congo. Moreover, the increased exports and
transit traffic allowed by the expanded transport capacity will be an important
source of foreign exchange.
5.12      The whole proposed ATC investment program for the period 1974-78,
including the realignment and complementary investments in the CFCO, ports
and river services, was evaluated similarly considering incremental costs and
benefits with regard to a reference case which assumes sufficient capital
expenditure to maintain the ATC transport system in operation under present
efficient levels without, however, any capacity-increasing investment. This
evaluation yielded a rate of return of 23%.
E.   Sensitivity Analysis
5.13      A sensitivity analysis was carried out to assess the effect of
potential changes in assumptions for critical benefit items. Changes in in-
vestment costs were not analyzed, as bids for the main civil works contract
(representing 80% of the total realignment costs) have already been received
and evaluated. The sensitivity analysis was applied to: (a) variations in
traffic under the reference case due to changes either in line capacity or
assignments in traffic priorities; and (b) changes in traffic demand on the
railway regarding Cameroon and Central African Republic timber. The economic
returns obtained from this exercise varied from 7% to 34% in the comparison
of the realignment with the reference case, from 8% to very large when the
realignment was compared to the combination of road and rail, and from 14%
to 39% for the total proposed ATC investments compared to the pertinent
reference case (Table 13). In the context of a sensitivity analysis, these
rates of return are satisfactory.



- 21 -
6. FINANCIAL EVALUATION
A.  Background
6.01      The proposed project is designed to reduce unit operating costs on
the ATC system through technical and operational improvements, and to in-
crease capacity to meet the anticipated growth in traffic demand. The object
of the financial analysis is to determine whether and how the project can be
financed without severely affecting ATC's future financial position, and to,
establish whether and under what conditions it will provide an acceptable
financial rate of return.
6.02      The financial tables (14 through 21) have been prepared in current
CFAF francs under inflation assumptions shown in Table 14 in order to allow
a realistic assessment of ATC's cash position during and after implementation
of the Project. Tables showing breakdowns of operating revenues and working
expenses have been prepared for the years following 1974 both in constant
and current CFA francs. All proposed targets are based on current prices.
B.   Past Finances, 1970-75
6.03      ATC produced its first income accounts and balance sheets as of
December 31, 1970. The 1970 consolidated operations resulted in a net
surplus of about CFAF 400 million, with each operating section showing
profits. However, ATC's consolidated finances deteriorated steadily
through 1973, in spite of an average 50% tariff increase implemented in three
stages between February 1973 and November 1974. ATC's income account from
1970 to 1975 is summarized below; details are in Table 15 (operating revenue),
Table 16 (working expenditure), and consolidated in Table 17.
(in CFAF billion)
1970       1971        1972       1973      1974    1975
Operating Revenue          5.7         6.2        6.6         7.0      9.6    10.0
Working Expenditure        4.3         5.1        5.5         5.9       7.3     8.7
Cash Generation            1.4         1.1        1.1         1.1      2.3      1.3
Depreciation               0.8         0.9        1.1         1.4       1.7     1.8
Net Operating Revenue      0.6         0.2        0.0        (0.3)     0.6    (0.5)
Interest Charges           0.2         0.2        0.3         0.4      0.6      0.7
Net Surplus (loss)         0.4          -         (0-3)      (0.7)     0.0    (1.2)
Working ratio (%)         76          82         82          84        75      87
Operating ratio (X)       90          97        100         104       93      105
6.04      Except for the Port of Pointe Notre, and in 1974 the CFCO Railway,
all sections of ATC suffered increasing operating losses between 1971 and 1974,
because of poor traffic performance and inadequate tariff policies. The above-
mentioned tariff increase and the implementation of the first phase of the
1974-1978 investment plan were expected to improve ATC's 1975 financial per-
formance. Unfortunately, the slump in the timber market reduced timber traffic



- 22 -
from an anticipated 520,000 tons to about 360,000 tons in 1975, causing a
shortfall of budgeted revenues of about CFAF 1 billion. Also, operating costs
exceeded forecasts by about 8.5% in 1975, mainly because of a Government-
ordered salary increase. The significant shortfalls in ATC's net revenue
between 1970 and 1975 resulted in steadily deteriorating working capital
levels, culminating in assets/current liabilities and liquid assets/current
liabilities ratio of 0.7 and 0.4 respectively. Details are presented in
Tables 18 (balance sheet) and 19 (selected financial ratios).
C.   Financial Position 1976-1979
6.05      While it appears fully justified to assume that the world timber
market will recover from its present slump and that traffic will establish new
high levels by the end of the 1970's, there is a risk that the rate of
recovery over the next three years will not be sufficient to provide ATC with
needed revenues, with the result that ATC's financial position will continue
to be difficult. To alleviate this situation, the Government, in consulta-
tion with the Bank, decided on February 8, 1976 to:
(a) waive repayment of the sales tax collected by ATC for
repayment to the Treasury. This measure includes taxes
collected by ATC since January 1, 1975, and will be
maintained as long as required to rehabilitate ATC's
financial position;
(b) exempt ATC from the payment of custom duties on imported
materials starting January 1, 1976; and
(c) allow ATC to increase its tariffs by about 17% by March 1,
1976 on domestic traffic and by May 1, 1976 on transit
traffic (after consultation with other countries affected).
ATC's finances should also improve in the near future as a result of measures
recently taken by the Government to raise the productivity of public enter-
prises and limit increases in their expenditures, mainly by restricting staff
appointments and wage increases. The forecast income statements of ATC from
1976 to 1979 and relevant financial ratios, taking into account implementa-
tion of the above measures, are given in Tables 15-17 and Table 19, and
summarized below:

The largest item in the working expenditures shown above is for staff
expenses which been calculated assuming: (a) staff numbers remaining fairly
constant; (b) wage increases being limited to inflation levels except for 5%
annual promotion increases; and (c) Improvements in staff productivity being
as forecast in Annex 5. Interest charges assume that the proposed Bank Loan
includes capitalization of interest up to US$3 million equivalent during proj-
ect implementation to help ease ATC's debt service burden.
6.06      A further 10% increase in tariffs beyond the 17% scheduled for the
current year, will be necessary in 1978 to allow ATC to achieve annual rates
of return on average net fixed assets in use shown in the table in para 6.05
above. These returns together with the recent Government measures, will
allow ATC to: (a) cover its vorking expenses; (b) progressively restore
working capital; (c) finance its share of the investment plan- and (d) service
its debt.   ATC has agreed to take necessary measures to achieve
an overall rate of return on net fixed assets in use of at least 6Z in 1977
and 8% in the years 1978 to 1980. These rates of return We to be calculated
without taking into account the Government's financial assistance to ATC in
the form of a tax waiver (para. 6.05).
6.07      All previous increases in ATC's tariffs have resulted from finan-
cial pressures rather than from a deliberate policy based on the cost of
transport. In contrast, and in recognition of the fact that a positive
policy is now required, agreement has been reached between the Government,
ATC, and the Bank that ATC will: (a) implement a cost-based tariff system
by December 31, 1978; and (b) increase tariffs as required to meet the above
mentioned financial criteria and offset inflationary pressures.
6.08      As shown in Table 15, all sections of ATC (except for the River
Transport Department which is especially sensitive to timber traffic), are



- 24 -
expected to generate net operating profits by 1977 under present traffic
assumptions, and to be financially viable by 1978. However, specific finan-
cial criteria for individual sections cannot be established until after im-
plementation of the new comprehensive costing system to be introduced under
the proposed project (paras. 3.08 and 6.07). ATC has therefore agreed that
financial criteria for its sections will be established for the years 1981
and thereafter in agreement with the Bank.
6.09      Following recommendations made by BCEOM/Sofrerail and discussions
with ATC's management, it was established in 1971 that ATC's working capital,
excluding cash, should cover the following:
-    Stores:         14 months of working expenses for materials
-    Receivables:   25% of annual operating revenue
-    Creditors:      25% of annual working expenditure, excluding
staff and head office costs
-    Debt service:  30% of debt service due in the following
financial year.                                   -
Since that time, ATC has been unable to generate enough funds to pay for its
investments; it therefore drew funds from its working capital which conse-
quently dropped far below the desired level. The Government and ATC have
now agreed that ATC's working capital will be progressively improved to meet
the above requirements by 1981.

The above summary includes the cost of the proposed CFCO realignment and the
grants and loans required to finance it. The table shows that ATC's overall
financial position will improve only slightly in 1976-79, in spite of the
significant Government assistance.   It also shows that bank overdrafts up to
CFAF 1.7 billion will be necessary in 1976/77. The Government has agreed
under the proposed project to provide overdraft facilities to ATC as
necessary.
6.11      The Government is aware of the fragility of ATC's cash position in
the near future. Therefore, in order to prevent any shortage of funds which
would jeopardize implementation of the entire 1974-78 investment plan, it
has provided assurances that: (a) ATC will not have to provide any funds
for the CFCO realignment during its construction; and (b) in case of any
shortfall, the Government will provide any funds necessary to assure imple-
mentation of the entire investment plan and to service all the debts of ATC
until December 31, 1980. In Addition, ATC has agreed to set up a separate
accounting system for the CFCO realignment along lines to be agreed with the
Bank.
6.12      ATC's fixed assets as of December 31, 1972 have been revalued by
consultants BCEOM/Sofrerail and incorporated into the 1974 accounts. ATC
has agreed that it will: (a) carry out a further revaluation of its fixed
assets in 1978; (b) thereafter, review the situation of its fixed assets in
consultation with the Bank at five-year intervals or less, and if necessary,
undertake revaluations from time to time in agreement with the Bank. ATC has
also agreed to the stipulation that income and revenue targets required to
control its finances after 1980 will be fixed periodically following discus-
sions with the Bank. The rates of return specified in the table in para. 6.05
above assume the revaluation of assets in 1978, and that assets acquired during
a given year will be entered in the accounts at current value of that year.

6.14      Due to the foreseen difficult financial situation of ATC, it has
been agreed between ATC, the Government and the Bank that during each year of
the project, ATC will provide the Bank by November 15 at the latest with
estimated financial results for the current year and the draft budget for the
following year, to enable the Bank to offer comments before its presentation
for approval by ATC's Board and the Government. It was further agreed that,
should the necessity arise, ATC and the Government would promptly agree with
the Bank on remedial action. Such action could consist of;(a) changes in ATC
tariffs or tariff policies; (b) reduction of current expenditures; (c) measures
aimed at restoring or improving productivity; and (d) action toward obtaining
Governmental financial assistance.



- 27 -
D.   Financial Position 1980-86
6.15      ATC's financial position can be expected to continue improving
following the implementation of planned investments over 1974-80 provided
that: (a) operational targets are achieved; (b) traffic projections, mainly
for timber, materialize; and (c) a cost-based and commercially competitive
tariff system is implemented. These factors are reflected in the following
table which summarizes ATC's income accounts and balance sheets for selected
years:
