Underlying assumptions used in preparing the income account were the follow-
ing: (a) tariffs increased by 10% every two years starting in 1980;
(b) number of staff remaining fairly constant; (c) wage increases limited to
inflation except for annual promotions; (d) inflation assumptions on wages,
materials, and services listed in Column 1 of Table 14; (e) improvement
in productivity as forecasted in Annex 5. Table 16b shows the breakdown in
percentage of the working expenses of ATC and its four operating sections
as a result of the above assumptions. ATC should be able to finance its
investments until 1986 from its own funds, provided conditions listed in para.
6.15 materialize.



- 28 -
6.16      The most noticeable effect of implementing the proposed Investments
during 1974-85 will be the accumulation of uncommitted cash, requiring a
decision by the Government and ATC as to whether ATC should: (a) finance or
participate in financing further substantial investments; (b) reduce tariffs;
or (c) pay substantial dividends to the Government. Agreements have been
reached that the use of any excess funds generated by ATC will be determined
after consultation between ATC, the Government, and the Bank.
E.   Sensitivity Analysis
6.17      Details of the sensitivity analysis of financial forecasts are
given in Annex 12. This analysis was conducted to test the impact on ATC's
financial position of a possible decrease in ATC's cash generation and/or
a possible increase in the cost of capital investments. The following
assumptions are used; (a) 20% decrease in gross operating revenue generated
by the timber traffic; (b) 10% increase in working expenditure; and (c) 10%
increase in the cost of capital investments, except for the realignment. The
analysis showed that using the above assumptions would result in a cumulated
shortage of cash of about CFAF 46 billion which could be overcome by the
cumulative effect of tariff increases roughly in line with inflation assump-
tions used in the main analysis (Table 14) ,and of additional borrowing of
about CFAF 8 billion in 1978/79 and up to about 40% of ATC's 1980-86 invest-
ments. The main result of the most pessimistic sensitivity assumption would
be: (a) a substantial increase in ATC's long-term debt service; and (b) a
delay in the accumulation of uncommitted cash. In order to prevent its
present and future borrowing expanding beyond a safe level, ATC has agreed
that its net revenue of each year will be more than 1.25 times its maximum
debt service of the following year, unless otherwise agreed with the Bank.
7.   AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.01      During negotiations on the proposed Loan, the following principal
items were discussed and agreed with the Government and ATC:
(i) the Government will consult with the Bank before undertaking
any major investments in the transport sector outside the ATC
investment program (para. 2.07);
(ii) the present level of technical assistance available to ATC, and
the responsibilities of technical assistants in line positions,
will not be reduced without prior approval of the Bank (para. 3.03);
(iii) the level of specified operating targets to be achieved for Pointe
Noire port, the CFCO railway, and river transport services (paras.
3.13 - 3.18);



- 29 -
(iv) the Government will make no change in the investment plan
for 1974-78 (except for minor items totalling not more than
CFAF 200 million annually) unless in agreement with the Bank
(para. 3.19); also, ATC has agreed to review with the Bank by
December 31, 1977, its investment program for 1979-85 to ascer-
tain any modifications required by conditions pertaining at
that time (para. 3.20);
(v) all arrangements for financing and procurement of goods and
services included in the ATC 1974-78 investment plan (paras. 4.10 -
4.13);
(vi) ATC will take necessary measures to achieve an overall rate of
return on net fixed assets in use of at least 6% in 1977 and 8%
in the years 1978 to 1980 (para. 6.06);
(vii) a cost-based tariff system will be implemented by December 31, 1978,
and tariffs will be increased as required to meet specified finan-
cial criteria and offs&t inflationary pressures (para. 6.07);
(viii) measures will be taken to progressively improve ATC's working
capital to meet agreed requirements by 1981 (paras. 6.09 and
6.14);
(ix) the Government has agreed that ATC will not have to provide any
funds for the CFCO realignment during its construction; also,
in case of any shortfall in funds, the Government will provide
the amounts necessary to assure implementation of the entire
investment plan and to service all ATC's debts until December 31,
1980 (para. 6.11); and
(x) ATC's net revenue of each year will be more than 1.25 times its
maximum debt service of the following year, unless otherwise
agreed with the Bank (para. 6.17).
7.02      During Loan negotiations, details of the supervisory arrangements
for the project were reviewed, and the Government, ATC, and the co-donors
have no objection to the Bank supervising both those project items in whose
financing it is participating, and also all other items, to ensure the
coordination and quality of the whole investment plan (para. 4.09).
7.03      A condition of effectiveness of the proposed Loan will be the
finalization of agreements on terms assuring financing of the CFCO realignment,
or assurances satisfactory to the Bank that funding for the works will other-
wise be forthcoming. An additional condition of effectiveness of the proposed
Loan will be the receipt of assurances satisfactory to the Bank from donors
whose contributions to the financing of the remaining items in the investment
plan have been agreed in principle but not yet secured. (para. 4.110).



- 30 -
7.04      The proposed project is suitable for a Bank Loan of US$38 million
to ATC with the guarantee of the Government of the People's Republic of the
Congo. The Loan term will be 25 years including five years of grace, at an
interest rate of 8-1/2% p.a.
March 9, 1976.

ANNEX 1
Page 1
CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
ATC - Training and Technical Assistance
A. Training
1.        ATC provides technical training in the following categories:
(i) for apprentices and other newly recruited staff at schools
in Pointe Noire, Loubomo, and Brazzaville;
(ii) in-service training for personnel who have completed
apprenticeships;
(iii) upgrading training for older staff to develop greater compe-
tence in their specialties; and
(iv) for administrative and middle-management personnel.
2.        In addition to the training provided under (i) and (ii) above, the
best apprentices from the mechanical workshop training school at Pointe Noire
are sent to the regional training school in Douala (Cameroon) for three years
of further study; in 1973, 11 ATC staff were enrolled. As a next step, the
most successful graduates of the Douala school, as well as other specialists,
go to France to attend training ccurses for higher technicians organized
through the Office Central des Chemins de Fer d'Outre-Mer (OFEROM) and con-
ducted by the French National Railways (SNCF). An additional source of
training is that provided by the manufacturers of new equipment items such
as locomotives, river craft, etc., as they often have to provide the required
after-sales service and training locally for about two years.
3.        ATC's long-term needs for efficient skilled manpower can be met
adequately if training provided in the Centre de Formation Professionnelle
Generale (CFPG) at Pointe Noire is supplemented by on-the-job and skill-
upgrading courses. However, the Director of CFPG is not authorized to
organ;ize such courses or to designate staff to attend them. Moreover,
the CFCO management set-up does not include a Personnel Officer who could
be responsible for recruitment, maintaining personnel records, staff develop-
ment, and organizing part-time courses for junior- and middle-level personnel.
These problems have resulted in the now virtual non-existence of in-service
and upgrading training in the maintenance of mechanical equipment. In-service
training-and refresher courses are carried out in traffic operations, track
and works, and telecommunications, but they are not well organized or co-
ordinated; this results in low staff morale, as the possibilities for career
advancement are not clearly defined. It is therefore essential that all
training activities be reorganized under a central authority.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
4.        Additional training needs include the following;
(i) some increase in the facilities for all the training categories
mentioned in para. 1 above;
(ii)  relocation of the track and works training school from Iouboimo
to Pointe Noire which has better administrative and practical
facilities;
(iii) extension of the Brazzaville school to train staff for the
marine workshops and river craft;
(iv) technical assistance for setting up instructor training courses
and to train local counterparts; and
(v) technical assistance to arrange in-service staff development
courses (to eventually replace part-time courses for middle-
management personnel now provided to Government organizations
by a French training institution).
B.   Technical Assistance
5.        Expatriate technical assistance is provided for all ATC operations,
for on-the-job training, and for teaching at the various schools. The level
of this technical assistance is shown in Table 1. Until mid-1972, the greater
proportion of the experts were employed on operations, particularly on the
CFCO. Since then, however, some of the technical assistants were replaced
by local personnel, and others were moved to training positions. The con-
sequence of this has been a decline in ATC's overall operating efficiency,
particularly on the CFCO, and it is therefore vital that increased technical
on-the-job training be provided in certain of the railway departments (mechan-
ical workshops, track maintenance, traffic operations, telecommunications
and signalling, and accounting) until a sufficient number of adequately train-
ed local personnel are available.
C.   Manpower and Equipment Requirements
6.        Details of the technical assistance and training equipment require-
ments to cover the needs identified above are given in Attachment 1 to this
Annex; an outline of terms of reference for the technical assistance services
is in Attachment 2.  The total costs are estimated at about US$4.6 million,
comprising US$3.8 million for technical assistance, and US$0.8 million for
training equipment.



ANNEX 1
Attachment 1
Page 1
CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
ATC - Details of Technical Assistance and Training Requirements
A.   ATC
1.        ATC Headquarters - Pointe Noire
(a) One personnel officer for 2 man-years to be responsible for
staff development, personnel records, and for initiating training
programs for accountants, administrative staff, and middle-level
management personnel;
(b) One expert for 2 man-years to set up a program of refresher
courses for staff development and training of local counter-
parts; and
(c) Three experts for about 2.5 man-years each for financial
management and accounting.
2.        ATC Training Center - Pointe Noire
(a) Equipment required to meet increased enrollment and improvement
in quality of training; and
(b) One expert in pedagogy and methodology for 2 man-years to set
up training courses for local instructors.
B.   CFCO Railway
1.        Operational Department
(a) Re-planning of existing facilities to allow improved working
conditons, including one extra classroom;
(b) Specialized equipment to be used for in-service training;
(c) One expert for 2 man-years to assist the Chief of the Department
in improving train operations and freight-car control and in
improving and expanding commercial activities; and
(d) Two experts for 2 man-years each mainly to work in the field
with local counterparts on inspection and training of staff at
outlying stations.



ANNEX 1
Attachment 1
Page 2
2.        Mechanical Department
(a) One additional classroom;
(b) One expert for 2 man-years to introduce and organize improved
workshop methods; and
(c) Two experts for about 2 man-years each to help introduce and
implement methodical maintenance for locomotives and rolling
stock. (These will be in addition to the six experts for a
total of about 7 man-years which CCCE has agreed to finance).
3.        Permanent Way Department
(a) Relocation of the track-maintenance school from Dolisie to Pointe
Noire;
(b) Equipment and tools for track-maintenance school;
(c) One expert for 2 man-years attached to the Chief Engineer's
Office to prepare methodical programs for track and building
maintenance, and to assist in costing and budgeting;
(d) Two experts for about 2 man-years each to work in the field with
local counterparts in inspection and staff training.
4.        Signalling and Telecomunications Department
(a) Additional facilities of one classroom, one room for workshop
practice, and one office;
(b) Additional equipment required to meet increased enrollment and
improvement in quality of training;
(c) One expert for 2 man-years to introduce new equipment and working
and maintenance methods, and to train staff in the field.
C.   Port of Brazzaville
1.        Marine Workshops
(a) Training facilities for skill-upgrading comprising one classroom
and one room for workshop practice;
(b) Equipment and tools for training purposes; and
(c) Three training experts for 2 man-years each to establish a training
school for technicians, mechanics, and workers involved in river
transport.

2.        School for Seamen
(a) One additional classroom; and
(b) Equipment and tools for training purposes.

Attachment 2

CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
ATC - Draft Outline of Terms of Reference for Technical Assistance
A. Introduction
To assist ATC in improving its operations, technical assistance
involving 17 experts for about 40 man-years will be required. The tasks
of the individual experts are described below.
B. Scope of Work for Technical Assistants
Personnel Officer (ATC)
2.        The Personnel Officer will be responsible to the General Manager
of ATC, and will be in charge of all recruitment of ATC staff. He will be
responsible for establishing a manpower plan for ATC to be completed by 1977.
He will prepare long-range manpower and staff development programs (including
development of Congolese instructors), and will see that appropriate mea-
sures are taken to realize them. He will arrange staff exchanges or trans-
fers between all sections of ATC, as required, under a balanced staff policy.
He will keep up-to-date personnel records for all ATC staff, and will in
particular be responsible for ATC's administrative staff and their develop-
ment, and for organizing part-time courses for junior- and middle-level
personnel. The Personnel Officer will also be responsible for ATC staff
in regional training programs, and will supervise staff enrolled in courses
abroad.
Expert for Refresher Courses (ATC)
3.        The expert will, in close cooperation with the Personnel Officer,
organize the timing of refresher courses now under the responsibility of
separate departments, thus relieving departmental instructors from time-
consuming administrative work and problems involved in releasing staff for
training. The expert will be expected to ensure efficient utilization of
installations, equipment, and instructors. His work program will also in-
clude the development of Congolese instructors.
Financial and Accounting Experts (ATC)
4.        The team will comprise a financial expert, an accountant,
and a costing and tariffing expert. They will be expected to: (i) improve
financial and statistical information required by management for planning
purposes; (ii) adapt the OCAM accounting plan to ATC's requirements; and



ANNEX I
Attachment 2
Page 2
(iii) prepare and assist in implementing a comprehensive costing system and
a cost-based system of tariffs. They will train Congolese staff in all
aspects of their work.
Expert in Pedagogy and Methodology (ATC)
5.        The expert will work in close cooperation with the Personnel Officer
and the heads of the CFPG and other training schools in Pointe Noire, Loubomo,
and Brazzaville, to develop suitable curricula and introduce training courses
in pedagogy and training methodology for technically-qualified ATC personnel
being trained as instructors. He should have an electro-mechanical back-
ground, and experience in instructor-training. In addition to the above
function, the expert will prepare one or more local staff to carry on his
instructor-training function on completion of his contract.
Expert as Assistant to the Operating Manager (CFCO)
6.        The expert will assist in improving train operations and freight-
car control, and in improving and expanding CFCO's commercial activities.
He will organize and assist in implementing an improved system for controlling
receipts, and will also help in training office staff.
Experts for Field Work and On-line Training (CFCO)
7.        The two experts will concentrate on the supervision of operating
and commercial staff in stations, and will carry out on-the-job training in
stations along the line. They will also promote traffic and foster good
relationships with customers.
Experts for Rolling Stock Maintenance (CFCO)
8.        The two experts will organize and assist in the implementation
and methodical maintenance methods for railway rolling stock, and will
develop programs to ensure better supply of spare parts. They will work
in close cooperation with the expert for workshop organization, and in
collaboration with the CCCE-financed SOFRERAIL consultant team involved in
locomotive maintenance. The experts will also participate in staff train-
ing.
Expert for Workshop Organization (CFCO)
9.        The expert will be expected to:  (i) organize and assist in
implementation of better workshop methods and planning; (ii) determine
work force requirements and supplies of spares and materials; and (iii)
establish a system for controlling workshop costs. The expert will also
participate in workshop training for ATC staff.



ANNEX 1
Attachment 2
Page 3
Permanent Way Expert (CFCO)
10.       The expert will:  (i) organize and assist in the implementation
of data collection; (ii) prepare methodical maintenance programs; (iii)
check the progress of works; (iv) determine equipment and work force
requirements; and (v) establish cost budgets for track and building main-
tenance, renewals, and improvement works. He will also train office staff
in permanent way maintenance procedures.
Permanent Way Experts (CFCO)
11.       The two experts will work in the field to assist local Congolese
supervisors, and to be responsible for the practical implementation of
methodical maintenance programs for the railway's track and on-the-job
training.
Signalling and Telecommunications Expert (CFCO)
12.       The expert will organize and assist in improving maintenance on
existing equipment, and in introducing new equipment. He will determine
work force requirements and assist In on-the-job training along the line
as well as in giving courses.
Vocational Training Experts (River Transport)
13.       The three experts will plan and assist in establishing a training
school for technicians, mechanics, and workmen involved in maintaining
ATC's river transport and river navigation fleet. One expert will be
responsible for organizing and giving the theoretical courses, developing
curricula for the different trades, and assisting the management of the
port and river transport sections of ATC in formulating staff development
plans. Two mechanics (one for diesel engine maintenance and one for other
workshop activities) will carry out on-the-job training in the proposed new
workshops at Brazzaville.

CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
ATC - Property Details
A. Headquarters
1.        ATC has a new multi-story office block at Pointe Noire for use
by all Headquarters' sections.
B. Port of Pointe Noire
2.        The port is an artificial one on a sandy bay, and protected from
the Atlantic surf by stone boulder moles; the area enclosed is about 84
ha. The entrance channel has a depth of 12 m at low tide, and the harbor
area 11 m; the range of tide is about 1.8 m. There is a littoral drift
from south to north, and occasional dredging has to be carried out to
maintain the required depths.
3.        There are five general cargo berths (alongside depth varying
from 7.3 to 9.15 m), two berths for timber and one for bulk oil (all depths
8.45 m), a berth for manganese export (depth 10.2 m), and a shallow draft
wharf for fishing vessels. Transit and storage sheds are provided for
general cargo2and timber (both sawn and plywood), with a total area of
some 80,000 m . Also in the port area are privately operated bulk instal-
lations for petrol and oils, export molasses, and fish freezing. There are
large areas (totalling some 14 ha) for timber stacking, sorting, and prep-
aration. At the manganese berth, COMILOG has its own stock area adequate
for about 250,000 tons of ore, and a bulk-handling installation for un-
loading railway cars and loading ships. The port area is adequately served
by both roads and railway.
4.        There are no wharf cranes, and all cargo is worked by ships'
derricks and mobile cranes. All shore-handling equipment, including that
for timber, is provided by private handling agencies. The port has a
cutter suction dredger and related equipment for dredging, one ocean-going
tug, two tugs for harbor use, a pilot cutter, VHF telephone equipment, and
a slipway and two derrick cranes. The fixed assets and equipment are
reasonably maintained.
5.        Construction of a new 9 ha area for timber-log stacking and han-
dling was completed in 1974 with financing from FAC/CCCE. The area is pro-
vided with a shallow draft wharf and cranes for loading heavy logs onto
pontoons, and with ramps for launching floating logs into the sea. A second
phase of reclamation, planned for 1976/77, will add a further 6 ha to the
stacking area.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
6.        A new berth being constructed with FED financing will provide
greater depth for bulk manganese carriers (11.6 m for 43,000-ton ships,
and later 13.2 m for 65,000-ton ships). Completion of this berth is ex-
pected by early 1976, and the existing manganese berth will be converted for
general cargo use.
7.        Outside the port area on the main sea coast, CPC has a railway
car offloading plant, bulk storage shed, and long qea jetty for the ship-
ment of bulk potash, with a capacity of some 1.5 million tons of potash
per year. However, the output of potash so far is only about 400-500,000
tons per year, so the installation is now under-utilized. The possibility
has been examined of using it for other bulk traffic, particularly for the
local oil development, but the exploration enterprise ELF decided to locate
all its sea installations near the offshore field, about 20 km south of
Pointe Noire.
C.   CFCO Railway
8.        The CFCO is about 510 km long with a gauge of 1.067 m.  The rail-
way was constructed between 1921 and 1934 at minimum cost, with a narrow
roadbed, steep gradients (2.2% uncompensated), and sharp curves (100 m
nominal radiusl but many actually 75-80 m over two mountain ranges, one
(the Mayombe) near the coast, and the other towards Brazzaville.   The
foreseen traffic capacity was probably some 500,000 net tons per year,
a level reached in 1955. The COMILOG manganese traffic which started in
1962, travels about 285 km on its own railway from the Gabon-Congo border,
then joins the CFCO at Mont Bello to travel a further 200 km to Pointe
Noire; manganese traffic has built up to over 2 million net tons p.a. at
present. Other traffic, particularly timber for export, has increased on
the CFCO as well, while the potash mine developed by CPC at about 45 km
from Pointe Noire, started production in 1970. Total volume carried in
1975 was about 3.8 million tons.
9.        The increased traffic, carried on heavier trains with heavier
axle loads, has led to considerable problems between Pointe Noire and
Dolisie, particularly over the Mayombe mountain section. These problems
include failure of the track foundation, high rate of rail wear, breakage
of rail joints, and collapse of the superstructure of concrete arch via-
ducts; they have in turn caused major and continuing maintenance problems
with the track and equipment, numerous accidents, considerable train de-
lays, and in recent years, restrictions on the amount of traffic carried.
Over 1960-66, heavier track material was laid on the sections from Pointe
Noire to Loubomo and Mont Bello, and in 1968-70, the alignment and track
on Pointe Noire-Holle (57 km) were improved to an adequate standard. For
the main and difficult mountain section Holle- IoUbomo, however, the then
ATEC and the Bank Group concluded in 1968 that very substantial improve-
ment of the existing conditions would be necessary. As a result, the
UNDP agreed to finance a feasibility study which was carried out by con-
sultants Tecsult.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
10.       This study was completed in 1972, and was followed by final
engineering (financed by IDA Credit 297-COB) of the recommended solution,
namely the improvement of about 18 km of the existing alignment, and the
construction of about 88 km. of completely new route. The new construc-
tion involves heavy earthworks through difficult and densely forested
country, with lengthy tunnelling and bridging, and all at considerable
cost; but no reasonable alternative is feasible while continuing to main-
tain the traffic flow under local operating conditions.
11.       The sections Loubomo-Mont Bello-Brazzaville also need track improve-
ments, but these are not so critical and can follow in subsequent investment
plan periods.
12.       The locomotive fleet is all diesel; availability at about 60 per
cent is poor but is partly due to the arduous operating conditions, and part-
ly to design problems with two of the more recent types of locomotives. The
locomotive and rolling stock fleets are being increased, and the older units
replaced as required with financing mainly by CCCE and IDA Credit 297-COB.
Further replacements and some additional main-line locomotives and passenger
cars are propossed to carry the forecast traffic to 1978. Maintenance of
motive _power and rolling stock needs to be improved, and better workshop
facilities and provision of spare parts are also required.  With the help of
CCCE financing, ATC is at present expanding the main shops at Pointe Noire,
and reorganization of the maintenance operations which will include the pro-
vision of maintenance manuals and staff training started in 1975. The exist-
ing depots at Loubomo and Brazzaville will be reorganized.
13.       Station signalling, as well as train control and telecommunications
equipment, have been inadequate; also, loops at stations for crossing trains
have been too short. ATC is taking steps to improve these facilities (com-
pletion of longer sidings in 1974, of station signalling in 1976) with partial
financing provided by ADB and CCCE, and further improvement is proposed in
conjunction with execution of the major track realignment on Fourastie-Loubomo.
14.       Existing important concrete arch bridges and the 1.5 km long summit
tunnel on the Mayombe mountain section are in poor condition, and would re-
quire major reconstruction if they are not replaced by the proposed new align-
ment. Bridges and culverts on other sections are adequate. Buildings at the
main centers are satisfactory; at wayside stations improved facilities are
needed, and are being introduced as new stations are built or existing sta-
tions reconstructed.
D. River Navigation, Ports and Transport
15.       The principal river route from Brazzaville to Bangui (CAR) extends
about 1,200 km along the Congo and Oubangui Rivers. Subsidiary routes serving
northeastern Congo and southeastern Cameroon are about 3,800 km., making a
total of about 5,000 km of river routes operated by ATC.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
16.       Prior to 1969, the then ATEC maintained the navigation facilities
on the system, and also the principal river ports of Brazzaville and Bangui;
transport on the rivers was handled by private enterprise. After the estab-
lishment of ATC in 1969, CAR became responsible for Bangui port, and the
Congo and CAR jointly took over the principal private river operator; ATC
assumed responsibility for the Congo portion of the fleet, and a CAR state
enterprise, ACCF (Agence Centraficaine des Communications Fluviales) for the
other portion. Some small private shippers remain who deal wholly with tim-
ber.
17.       Maintenance and navigational aids on the Congo river are dealt
with by neighboring Zaire, while on the Oubangui, Sangha, and connected
rivers, the work is shared between ATC and ACCF. On the Congo and Oubangui
rivers, the limiting depths at normal annual minimum levels, between February
and May are approximately 1.80 m and 1.00 m respectively; on the Sangha river,
the limiting depth is about 0.85 m as far as Ouesso, and lower upstream. At
periods of abnormal low river levels, such as occurred on the Oubangui in
February to April 1974, there can be complete interruptions to all river
transport. Programs for improvements in the river channels, prepared with
advice from the Laboratoire National d'Hydraulique (France), are intended
to eventually increase the normal ruling depth in the upper rivers to 1.20 m.
ATC has three dredgers and some rock-breaking work barges; additional survey,
dredging, and rock-breaking equipment for this work and for maintenance and
channel-working generally, is now being supplied to ATC and ACCF, with finan-
cing by FED.
18.       Brazzaville has two main port areas which are served by both road
and rail; one is for general cargo and heavy logs and sawn timber carried
on barges, and the other for handling floating logs. The former has a main
wharf wall 520 m long equipped with 11 portal cranes, three transit sheds,
mobile equipment, and with a large open stacking area for sawn timber and
logs; there are adjacent facilities for handling heavy lifts, bulk oil,
and passengers. The main wharf is being extended a further 250 m under FED
financing. The second port area has a wharf 250 m long with four portal
cranes, mobile equipment, and an extensive stacking ground for transferring
logs from river to railway. There is a small port at Ouesso with a 60 m
wharf, two fixed cranes, two transit sheds, and a stacking area; there are
minor installations at Makona, Bourdji, Fort Rousset and Mossaka.
19.       The ATC river transport fleet consists of 1 passenger ship, 48
tugs of various sizes, and 73 barges for general cargo, timber, bulk oil,
and passengers. An additional 17 tugs (mainly small horsepower), and 12
barges financed by ADB, are in course of supply; ADB is also examining the
provision of a further 7 tugs and 19 barges under a second loan.
20.       The ATC fleet is well maintained, and new units are adequately
designed and constructed. There are reasonable marine workshops at Brazza-
ville which have been adequate until now, but reorganization of operations



ANNEX 2
Page 5
and expansion of facilities are proposed to cater for the enlarging fleet.
Adjoining the floating timber port is another marine workshop originally
built by the Republic of China for the construction of vessels with timber
hulls; this workshop has been administered as a separate state enterprise
with ATC being obliged to take over and operate the units produced, although
not in favor of timber hulls because of the maintenance problems involved.
The facility was transferred by the Government in 1975 for minor industrial
use, instead of for ships.
E.   General
21.       Further details about the equipment in use on all the sections of
ATC are given in Annex 3.

SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
ATC - Operational Details
A.  Port of Pointe Noire
1.        The port orgainzation has the normal sections for administration,
pilotage, shore operations, and maintenance of equipment and fixed assets;
the accounting services is provided by ATC headquarters. All stevedore and
shore handling of cargo was carried out by private enterprise, including in
particular all the stacking, sorting, and shipment of timber. These opera-
tions will now be carried out by new jointly-owned enterprises (see para. 4).
2.        Data on port shipping, cargoes, and output for 1968-1974 are given
in Table 4. The number of ships using the port has remained static, but
the volume of cargo per ship has increased over the period.  The total cargo
handled also increased until 1971 (mainly manganese, timber, and bulk oils),
but has remained static since then. Non-floatable timber logs, sawn timber,
and plywood are loaded on ship at either the five general cargo berths or
at the two timber berths; floatable timber logs are put in the sea and loaded
over the seaward side of ships at berths, or overside on ships moored in
the harbor area.  The new timber log facilities completed at end - 1974, will
enable almost all log loading to be done over the ships' side and at the two
timber berths, and this in turn will provide greater capacity for general
cargo at the other berths.
3.        The port normally works a single shift of eight hours six days
a week, with a half shift on Sundays; an occasional second shift is worked
if required, at an extra charge to the ship. The resulting annual tonnage
of dry cargo (excluding manganese ore) handled per berth and per meter of
wharf is reasonable, but could be greater in view of the preponderance of
bulk timber traffic.
4.        There are two main problems at the port; one is that the private
cargo-handling agencies have been reluctant, due to political circumstances,
to invest in additional equipment; and the other is that port labor and
customs have not been in favor of operating a regular second shift. The
Government, ATC and the agencies have recently negotiated the formation of
new jointly-owned handling enterprises with technical direction remaining
with the agencies, to deal initially with timber in 1975 but to be extended
later to cover all cargo-handling in 1976. These joint enterprises would
then be responsible for provision of the necessary equipment, again particularly
for timber-handling, and intended to be financed by CCCE. ATC expects that
the new port enterprises will be able to obtain the cooperation of the labor
syndicate and customs on improving cargo-handling operations, including the



ANNEX 4
Page 2
working of a regular second shift, particularly for timber-handling, as and when
required. Other aims are: (i) to remove long-term timber log storage
from the port to new storage areas adjacent to the new Pointe Noire rail-
way marshalling yard; and (ii) to maintain much fuller statistical data
on cargo operations than has been available to the port management to date.
The port facilities should then be adequate to meet forecast traffic re-
quirements until about 1980.
5.        The port is also used as a base for tuna fishing, and recently,
also as the base for offshore oil exploration and development some 20 km
to the south of Pointe Noire.
B.   CFCO Railway
6.     _  The principal traffic operations on the CFCO are COMILOG manganese
ore, CPO potash, and general traffic, principally timber.
7.        COMILOG operates its own trains over the CFCO from Mont Bello
to Pointe Noire; it has the use of up to three train paths each way per
day, and has been able to meet the increasing export tonnage by increasing
the motive power and bulk ore wagons per train. The present avera e net
train load is about 2,500 tons, and the gross load about 3,200 tons. The
limit of COMILOG capacity for ore, estimated at 2.6 million tons per year,
is controlled by the capacity of the 56 km ropeway linking the mine to the
railhead. The operating arrangements for COMILOG traffic on the CFCO and
also, conversely for CFCO traffic on the COMILOG railway, are covered by
agreements between ATC and COMILOG.
8.   The potash traffic is carried 47 km to Pointe Noire, and has required
about one train path per day so far. The bulk wagons are provided by CPC,
while the CFCO supplies the motive power. This section of the CFCO track
and stations has been improved, and the traffic does not cause any parti-
cular problem to the CFCO.
9.        Timber traffic reaches the CFCO as follows:  from southern Congo
and Gabon at Mont Bello and Iouboao, and from northern Congo, southern
Cameroon and CAR at Brazzaville.
10.       The increasing traffic (mainly timber) on the CFCO has been
carried partly by enlarging the fleet of diesel locomotives and freight
cars, and partly by increasing the size of the units. The increasing
weight of axle loads and of gross train loads, both with the COMILOG and
the CFCO traffic, have led to problems on the track between Holle and
Loubomo. These in turn have imposed limitations in operations because of
the loss of time involved with track maintenance, locomotive failures,
accidents, and derailments. Moreover, the problems are compounded by:
(i) lack of space at stations for train movements and to cross longer,
heavier trains; (ii) inadequate signalling, telecommunications, and train
control facilities; and (iii) low operating staff efficiency. ATC under-
took measures to improve items (i) and (ii) above, and after long delays



ANNEX 4
Page 3
in financing and execution, the work on (i) was finally completed at end-19749
and on (ii) will be completed in 1976. The question of improving staff effi-
ciency is part of the general need of ATC to expand and improve the teaching
programs, and to provide additional technical assistance for on-the-job
training.
11.       The CFCO capacity control lies in the Down or export direction
over the section Loubomo to Holle; the figure has been 0.9 million net tons
per year of CFCO traffic (i.e. excluding manganese ore traffic) since 1971,
as fixed by the number of daily train paths available for through CFCO
freight trains and the average load per train. The extension of crossing
stations and the improvement to signalling will enable longer heavier trains
to operate, but will not increase the number of train paths. The train
load improvement may increase the Down traffic figure to about 1.6 million
tons per year to 1978. Thereafter, continuing deterioration of the in-
frastructure without remedial works, would cause additional and increasing
restrictions on traffic capacity.
12.       The locomotive fleet is all diesel-powered.  Its availability is
poor and averaged 57% for locomotives, and 64% for railcars in 1974, due to
the arduous operating conditions, poor maintenance facilities and organization,
and the acquisition of some locomotives which require modifications to make
them suitable for heavy duty work in the Congo. ATC is at present expanding
the main workshop facilities and reorganizing the maintenance operations for
which technical assistance is required. With these measures, it should be
possible to increase by 1978 the average availability of locomotives and rail-
cars to about 76% (making allowance for the inherent design problems in two
of the recent locomotive types).
13.       Although increased technical assistance and training programs will
help improve operations, they will not have much impact on the CFCO capacity
problem. Accordingly, a new improved alignment is essential to produce
easier operating conditons and thereby improve traffic capacity on the
critical sections.
C.   River Ports and Transport
14.       The division of traffic to be carried by ATC and ACCF is con-
trolled by Agreements between Congo and CAR. The latest such Agreement of
May 1972 provides for transport as follows:
(a)  general and timber traffic via CAR, 80% by ACCF and 20%
by ATC;
(b)  bulk oil traffic via CAR, 100% by ACCF; and
(c)  all other traffic (Cameroon principally), 50% by ATC,
but traffic to and from the Ngoko River (the border between
Cameroon and Congo), 100% by ATC.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
15.       At Brazzaville, ATC is responsible for the trans-shipment of
cargo between river and rail wagons. ATC provides the wharves, transit
sheds, stacking grounds, cranes, and other handling equipment.
16.       ATC operations are reasonably efficient, but are dependent to
some extent on the vagaries of the river flows and levels; low river
flows are normal from January to June, but low levels can persist for
another two months. In 1970, about 45% of that year's traffic was carried
in January to June; but in 1971, only about 34% was carried over the same
period, due to the late rise in river levels. In 1974, there was complete
interruption of traffic on the Oubangui between February and May due to
abnormally low river levels.
17.       Transport of timber from the forest to collection points on the
rivers is undertaken by the logging companies, who also construct the
rafts of floating timber and load barges with timber that is too valuable
or heavy to float; the logging companies also load barges with timber sawn
into planks at the r sawmills. The rafts on the upper rivers are made up
of about 400-500 m  of logs, and are brought downstream by small tugs 3
(90-150 HP) to collecting points; there, larger rafts of up to 1,400 m
are formed for movement to Brazzaville. Barges are mostly about 250-ton
capacity, and are formed into convoys of about 1,500 tons propelled by
large pusher tugs (240 HP on the upper river, and 400 HP on the main
river). ATC has made the following assessment of requirements for timber
transport by river: (i) for each 65,000 tons (79,600 cu m) annually of
floating logs, an average of eleven small tugs (90-150 HP) are required
for the trip to Brazzaville; and (ii) for each 40,000 tons annually of
logs and sawn timber carried by barge, one tug of 240 HP, two tugs of
400 HP, and 14 barges of 250 ton capacity, are needed.
18.       According to the Conventions and Decrees under which logging
agreements are made, increasing proportions of certain types of timber
are required to be processed in the country of origin. As a result,
there are increasing quantities of sawn timber being transported by barge.
19.       Details of river traffic at Brazzaville and smaller ports over
1968-73 are given in Table 6. Overall, the total general traffic (ex-
cluding timber) increased to 1971, but has declined since then, althbough
the ATC portion has remained stable. The increase in traffic has been
entirely with timber, about 500% in five years. ATC's operational per-
formance both at Brazzaville port and on the river services have shown
some decline since 1971, and a reversal of this tendency is expected as a
result of the proposed project.

CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
Main Items in the Proposed Project
A. ATC Headquarters
Training
1.        Training facilities and methods for all sections of ATC need to
be improved, and this would require provision of additional classrooms and
equipment, as well as some reorganization. Technical assistance is also
needed, both for training and for op_rations,_particularly on the CFCO. A
total of about 40 man-years of technical assistance, requiring about 17
experts, is proposed for financing under the present project.
B.  Port of Pointe Noire
2.        Investments are mainly to complete ongoing items such as new timber
log handling facilities, new manganese berth and conversion of the existing
berth to general cargo operations, improved slipway facilities, and iredging
of the harbor area.
3.        The port development plan up to 1978 also originally envisaged the
start of consturction on: (i) a mole and facilities for fishing vessels, and
(ii) two additional deepwater berths for timber handling. Both these items
have been reported on and designed in outline by consultants BCEOM. The first
is an independent item, separate from the general through-traffic capacity
requirement of ATC. The case for it seemed doubtful in view of the unpredic-
table location of the fishing grounds, and the number of other fishing ports
on the West African coast. The case for the additional two deepwater berths
was also doubtful because it is considered possible to increase output with
additional equipment and more regular second shift working (Annex 4, paras.
3-4). In view of the above, the two items have been eliminated from the 1974-
78 Investment Plan.
C.   River Services
4.        At Brazzaville port, the only major item is the renewal of some
old portal cranes at the general cargo wharf which are required for the more
efficient handling of cargo, heavy timber logs, and packaged sawn timber.
Provision is also made for additional miscellaneous items of mobile handling
equipment, such as fork lift trucks, which are required to meet the increase
in timber traffic.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
5.        Expenditure on river navigation is to continue the work of
deepening the upper river channels and improving the navigational aids so
as to extend the annual seasons for traffic movements. The equipment re-
quired has already been provided under FED financing.
6.        The increase in the river transport fleet is planned by ATC in
conjunction with that of ACCF, having regard to the division of traffic
between the two agencies as agreed by the Governmedts of Congo and CAR. The
total timber traffic forecast through Brazzaville is also controlled by the
total capacity of the CFCO to 1978 to carry timber, and the balance of
capacity available after the CFCO caters for timber coming from south Congo
and Gabon. Table 8 sets out the total annual timber traffic, the amount
transported through Brazzaville, the division between ATC and ACCF and the
additional capacity being provided or proposed. Loans are now being nego-
tiated by ATC with the ADB, and by ACCF with KFW (Germany); if these are
implemented, there will be sufficient capacity through 1978, but a shortage
may arise by 1980. As it takes two years or more to obtain delivery of and
to erect the larger tugs and barges, orders for some additional equipment
may need to be placed by 1977. ATC proposes the purchase of 6 small tugs
150 HP, 3 Tugs 400 HP, 14 barges 250-ton capacity each; this would provide
a combined additional capacity of about 65,000 tons against a total additional
requirement at 1980 of about 265,000 tons. The position should be reviewed
about 1977.
7.        Expansion of the ATC fleet requires improvement of the marine
workshops to cater for the increased erection and maintenance work involved.
ATC has prepared a scheme for extending the slipway facilities, constructing
a wharf for work on units in the river, and reconstructing the present con-
gested workshop and spare parts facilities. The work is planned for execu-
tion in two phases over 1975-78, with ATC and Congo banks financing local
construction costs, and CCCE financing the costs of foreign equipment aind-
materials.
D.   CFCO Railway - General
8.        For track works, the proposed investments provide for the con-
tinuing rail renewals required, principally on the heavily curved section
Holle-Loubomo; completion of work on lengthening crossing loops and instal-
lation of signalling at selected stations to accommodate longer and heavier
trains; and provision of improved marshalling yard facilities at Pointe Noire
and Brazzaville, again to allow operation of larger freight trains. Local
costs are to be met by ATC, and foreign costs by CCCE.
9.        The main mechanical workshops are at present being enlarged and
reconstructed; provision is made for completion of this work, all at local
cost to be met by ATC and a loan from local banks. Another item for comple-
tion is the provision of improved vacuum brakes on existing freight cars,
again required for operation of the heavier trains proposed. Local costs
are to be met by ATC, and foreign costs by CCCE.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
10.       New locomotive and car purchases include 10 main line 1,800 HP
locomotives, and 10 passenger coaches, plus appropriate spare parts. Of the
10 main line locomotives, five are intended to replace seven 1,350 HP units
which are now 23 years old and in process of being scrapped as spare parts can
cannot be obtained; the other five are partly to cater for increase traffic
over 1974-78 (about 600,000 tons excluding manganese ore), and partly to
enable some of the older (18 years) 1,100 HP BB locomotives to be relegated
to shunting and local working duties, thus avoiding purchase of new larger
shunting units to serve the increasing traffic and heavier trains. Allowance
has also been made for improvement in availability of the existing fleet of
main-line locomotives from the average of 57 per cent achieved in 1974 to an
expected 76 per cent average in 1978 following improvement of workshop facili-
ties and re-organization of maintenance operations. Financing for the locomo-
tives and coaches has been agreed with CCCE.
E.   Realignment
11.       This major item is intended to replace and improve the existing sec-
tion of railway through the Mayombe mountain range between Holle and Loubomo
which is badly graded and aligned, and where the track material and infrastruc-
ture are in poor condition. Between Holle and Fourastie (about 18 km), minor
improvements will be carried out adjacent ot the existing route, but from
Fourastie to Loubomo (about 88 km), a complete new alignment will be construct-
ed. Due to the steep terrain and the narrow valleys towards the mountain
summit ridge, it is not possible to develop a completely surface line, and a
4.5 km summit tunnel is necessary. The new line will provide:
(i) a maximum grade of 1.5% (compensated for curvature) in the
import or Up direction, and 1 per cent in the export or Down
direction, against 2.2% and 1.6% (uncompensated) respectively
on the existing line;
(ii) 300 m radius minimum curvature, with transition curves, against
100 m nominal and 75-80 m actual;
(iii)  a stronger track, 46 kg/m rail instead of 36 kg/m., better
sleepers, and a full width ballast section of well graded and
good quality stone, against a narrow and inadequate section of
poor quality material; and
(iv) a roadbed 5.5 m wide plus side drains, against 3-4 m without
adequate side drainage.
Five new crossing stations will provide improved facilities to cross long
trains (main loop lengths of 900 m extendable to 1,400 m, against 500-800 m.)
The summit and other minor tunnels will be adequately lined, lit, drained,
and ventilated; the new bridges will be built to modern load and design
standards (the existing structures are in poor condition, and would require



ANNEX 6
Page 4
reconstruction if retained). Overall, train loads for large locomotives in
the export or Down direction could be increased from 1,000 net tons to 1,500
net tons, while the annual freight traffic capacity will become about 12
million tons (with possibility of further expansion) against about 5 million
tons for the existing line. The final engineering and detailed estimates
have been prepared by consultants Tecsult.

CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
Project Financing
1.        The project comprises the ATC Investment Plan for 1974-78.  A break-
down of the various items included in the plan and their costs are given in
Table 9A (investment plan excluding the CFCO realignment) and Table 9B (the
CFCO realignment). The total cost of the project net of taxes is CFAF 52.59
billion (including physical and price contingencies), of which the CFCO
realignment accounts for CFAF 33.48 billion.
CFCO Realignment
2.        The financing plan for the CFCO realignment was accepted at a meeting
of co-donors held in Brussels on December 17-18, 1975. Details of this finan-
cing plan are given in Table 1 to this Annex. The co-donors have reached
agreement on terms assuring financing of the realignment. Finalization of
these agreements, or assurances satisfactory to the Bank that financing will
otherwise be forthcoming, will be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed
Loan. Cost estimates are reasonably reliable, as bids have been received and
evaluated for the main civil works contract which represents about 80% of
the total costs of the realignment. The contract is expected to be awarded
to the low bidder in April 1976; civil works will start by about June 1976,
and are scheduled to be completed in about 31 months. Because some co-donors
have been unable to agree to unqualified joint financing, the main civil works
contract, as well as some of the other items included in the realignment, are
subject to parallel financing as discussed below.
3.        The main civil works have been divided into three parts to accoumo-
date the wishes of those donors wanting parallel financing. The first and
main part includes earthworks, structures, and laboratory testing which
will be financed jointly by the Bank, FED, FAC, CCCE and the Government.
Since the Government's contribution represents about 13% of the total cost
of this contract and since the contractor is requesting that 35% of the pay-
ments be made in local currency, the donors have agreed to finance the remain-
ing local costs. Disbursements under this contract will be in proportion to
the contributions of individual donors as follows: Bank 43%, FED 24%, FAC 15%,
CCCE 5%, Government 13%. The second component of the civil works comprising
the construction of tunnels and the supply and installation of tunnel ventilation,
will be financed entirely by a group comprising KDF, SFD and BADEA. The third
component consists of the supply and placing of ballast together with the track
laying, and will be financed by ADB (56%) and the Government (44%).



ANNEX 8
Page 2
4.        Other works and contracts included in the realignment will be
financed as follows:
(a) Supply and treatment of timber ties: Started in 1975, this
item is being financed by CIDA and the Government.
(b) Supply of rails: Bids for the rails will be called in 1976
as a single order for both sections Holl4-Fourastig-Loubomo,
on the basis of a loan from ADB.
(c)  Supply of rail fastenings:  This item will be jointly finan-
ced by the Bank, FED, FAC, CCCE and the Government in the same
proportions as for the earthworks, structures, and laboratory
testing (para. 3 above).
(d) Supply of turnouts: As the turnouts will be procured from a
French supplier for standardization reasons, CCCE will finance
this item.
(e) Signalling: Procurement for this item will be included in a
larger order already partly financed by CCCE.
(f) Telecommmunications: The Government and ADB will jointly fi-
nance the supply and installation of this equipment.
(g) Station buildings and housing: The KDF/SFD/BADEA group will
finance the foreign cost component, and the Government the
local cost component.
(h) Access roads: The roads were built in 1975 using CIDA and
Government funds.
(i) Technical supervision: As the consultants (Tecsult, Canada)
who carried out the feasibility study and detailed engineering
of the realignment have been retained to supervise the con-
struction works, this item is being financed by CIDA and the
Government
Cost estimates for the items listed above are based on recent contracts and
quotations.
5.        As the contract for the main civil works calls for advances to the
contractor for mobilization (20% of the contract amount) and materials and
equipment (up to 20% of the contract amount), and as some of the funds of the
donors (including the Bank) might not be available at the time these advances
are to be paid, BEAC has agreed to arrange for a loan to ATC of up to CFAF 2.0
billion for the first six months of the contract to meet any temporary finan-
cing shortfall.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
Investment Plan Excluding CFCO Realignment
6.        The cost of the items in the investment plan (excluding the CFCO
realignment) is estimated at CFAF 19.11 billion including price and physical
contingencies. The items shown for 1974 have already been carried out, and
those for 1975 have been or are being implemented. Much of the financing
has already been secured. The receipt of assurances satisfactory to the Bank
from donors whose contributions have been agreed upon in principle but not yet
secured, will be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed Loan. Exclu-
ding the contributions of the Government, ATC and the Bank, these assurances
would be required for about 25% of the total estimated cost of all items ex-
cluding the realignment. The financing plan for these items is shown in Table 2
of this Annex.
7.        An amount of US$ 3.8 million from the proposed Bank Loan will be
applied to financing ATC's technical assistance and training requirements.
Although some of the technical assistants will be working in ATC's operating
divisions, the complete amount is shown under the main heading of ATC Head-
quarters. All the other items in the investment plan, excluding the realign-
ment, will be procured in accordance with the individual procedures of the
co-donors involved. The donors have no objection to Bank supervision missions
checking on the implementation of these items to ensure the coordination and
quality of the entire investment plan.

ANNEX 9

CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
ATC Traffic Projections
A.   Timber Traffic
1.        The ATC timber traffic forecast must be seen in the context of the
overall market for tropical timber, the position of West Africa in this market,
and finally, the position of the Northern Congo and Southwestern Cameroon
regions and the CAR in the West African scene.
2.        Production of tropical hardwood decreased in 1974  and in 1975; how-
ever, this phenomenon reflected the world-wide economic recession, and is not
representative of the long-term trend. During the period 1962-73, imports of
tropical hardwoods grew world-wide at about 12% per annum. In Europe, the
major market for African timber, the growth rate was lower, averaging about 6%
per annum. However, there is considerable evidence that the European demand
was increasing rapidly throughout the period as shown by the average annual
growth rates of 3.5% in 1962-68, and 8% in 1968-73.
3.        West African timber exports did not keep pace with this growing
European demand. The annual increase in shipments averaged only 4% through-
out the 1962-73 period, and lagged well behind the rapid expansion in con-
sumption noted in the second half of the period. The main reason was a
restricted supply of accessible timber in certain countries in the region
as follows:
(a) Nigerian exports have been progressively diverted to growing
local demands.
(b) Ghana's output of timber has been controlled by the Govern-
ment on a relatively low-yielding cutting cycle in order
to conserve long-term timber supplies.
(c) Congo's timber exports grew rapidly during the early 1960's,
reflecting the exploitation of accessible coastal areas.
As these sources became depleted and timber development
depended on a limited road infrastructure further inland,
exports stagnated during the period 1964-68. In 1968,
Congo stopped allocating forest concessions in the South,
and held up allocations in the North pending the completion
of forest inventories. Log output declined significantly
as a consequence of this action.
(d) Gabon's accessible coastal forests have been largely al-
located and logged in recent years, and interior resources
lacked the transport infrastructure to permit large-scale
operations.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
However, those countries which chose to take advantage of the growing demand
and had an appropriate transport infrastructure did very well, indicating
that West African hardwoods were competitive in the European market. For
example, between 1962-73 Ivory Coast exports increased about 10% annually
from 1.3 to 4.1 million tons, exports from Cameroon increased even faster
from 0.2 to 0.9 million tons, and Liberian traffic flourished similarly.
4.        Over the next 5 to 10 years, world demand for tropical hardwoods
is expected to grow because of the continued severe shortage of quality
hardwood in the temperate zones. Projections for the major European market
for West African timber call for a 3.5-4.5% per annum increase between the
mid-1970's and 1985, as compared with 6% in the previous decade. Demand in
the United States, of less importance as a West Africa market, is expected
to increase at 6.5% per annum as compared with 10-15% in the past.
5.        Africa's high value timber is traditionally preferred in the
European market to that of other producers, and is demonstrably competitive
in price since the associate members of the EEC can benefit from preferential
trading conditions there. However, production in the Ivory Coast has peaked,
and for reasons outlined in para. 3 above, little additional capacity can
be expected from Ghana and Nigeria. The largest forest reserves are therefore
in the central areas of Zaire, northern Congo, eastern Gabon, southeastern
Cameroon, and southwestern CAR. Among these countries, Congo is the first in
West Africa to pass legislation and establish conditions for introducing large-
scale efficient processing industries. Measures to re-open timber allocation
procedures were started in 1974 with the issuance of invitations for bids from
concessionaires covering an area of one million ha in the north 1/. While
planning in Cameroon and CAR has not been undertaken in such detail, forestry
concessions are in the process of being let. Further, because of their liberal
forest policies and substantial reserves, these two countries have recently
-been attracting the attention of logging companies that are running out of
resources in the Ivory Coast and Nigeria, and if an adequate tranpsort infra-
structure could be assured, output could be increased substantially and rela-
tively quickly. It is therefore likely that timber traffic growth in Cameroon
and CAR will be more rapid than in the Congo. Although Zaire has substantial
forestry potential, its forest areas are rather inaccessible, and development
is hampered by lack of policy planning and of adequate transport facilities.
Hence, while the available resources are certainly sufficient to generate a
rapid growth in output, there is considerable doubt that the full potential
will be reached.
6.        In absolute terms, the major European market for West African
hardwoods shoild offer a total import potential for hardwoods of about 3
25 million m (roundwood equivalent) by 1985, as against 15.3 million m
1/   Although the results of these invitaions were disappointing, they are
expected to improve (a) once the prospective logging companies know that
the realignment project is underway and that ATC will have sufficient
capacity to carry the increased production, and (b) as the world timber
market starts to recover from the slump period.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
(roundwood equivalent) in 1973. By contrast, Bank-sponsored forestry
missions have estimated 1985 export production of the various West African
countries as follows:
(in million m  roundwood equivalent)

7.        The production constraints noted in the preceding paragraphs are
expected to limit exports from the Western group of African countries as a
whole to current levels, representing less than 30% of the anticipated
European demand. Even under the most optimistic production estimates, the
Central African group will produce only the equivalent of 60% of the balance
of the European requirements, and more likely, only 50% (assuming the lower
production range level for Zaire). Thus, in the context of the European
market where African timber enjoys the advantages of proximity and prefer-
ence, the major constraint would appear to be production capacity rather
than sales potential, and the above export projections appear reasonable.
8.        For those countries in the ATC service area (Cameroon, CAR, Gabon,
and Congo), the projected increases in exports over 1972-85 range from 60% -
70% in the case of Gabon, to 200% - 300% for CAR. The approximate annual
rate of increase for each country may be summarized as follows:
Gabon                       3.5% - 4.25%
Congo                          5% - 6.5%
Cameroon                    7.5% - 9.4%
CAR                            9% - 11%
The highest growth patterns apply to the Cameroon and CAR, which provide a
combination of liberal forest policies and substantial reserves. These
growth levels approximate that applying to the Ivory Coast over the past
decade. Congo's somewhat lower rate of growth recognizes the stricter
controls over the forest reserves and the consequent slower rate of granting
concessions. Gabon's potential is somewhat lower pending the development of
transport arteries into the less accessible inland areas.
91        The estimated exports of the four countries in 1985, converted from
m of roundwood equivalents to tons, may be summarized as follows:

TOTAL:                   5.2 - 6.1
The range in each country reflects possible variations in the essential
elements of access (transport availability) and in prices, as a function
of market conditions and the predominant types of hardwood in the regions
developed.
10.       The ATC timber traffic estimates for 1985 assume that all Congo
and most CAR traffic will move via the ATC system. In the case of the Congo,
about 80% (950,000 tons) of the traffic will originate in the north (served
by the Congo, Oubangui, and Sangha rivers), and about 20% (250,000 tons) from
the southeren interior as a result of Government policy and the extent of
timber reserves. Cameroon traffic for the ATC (400,000 tons) has been esti-
mated as that which could most cheaply be moved from the producing areas over
the ATC system even if roads (currently non-existent) were constructed in the
region to provide access to the Cameroon transport infrastructure (see "eco-
nomic watershed" in Annex 10). Gabonese timber traffic in 1985 has been
completely discounted, on the grounds that al such traffic would be diverted
to the Transgabonese railway upon its completion at that time. Thus, the
1985 ATC timber traffic would be as follows:
(in million of tons)
Cameroon                            0.4
CAR                                 0.4
Congo                               1.2
Gabon                               0.0
Total:                         2.0
The projections assume that the present slump in the world timber traffic will
end in 1977, with timber traffic fully recovering in 1978. Timber traffic from
Gabon and southeastern Congo is then assumed to stabilize, with Gabonese timber
being diverted to the Transgabon Railway upon its estimated completion in 1985.
Timber traffic from CAR is assumed to continue growing through 1985 at an aver-
age of 12% per annum (from 182,000 tons in 1978 to 400,000 tons in 1985), and
traffic from southeastern Cameroon at 15% per annum (from 146,000 to 400,000
tons). Given the possible development of alternative evacuation routes and the
uncertainties of projecting market conditions that far in the future, no further
growth is assumed for CAR and Cameroon beyond 1985. The growth of timber traffic
from northern Congo is projected to be rapid until 1985 (from 182,000 tons in
1978 to 950,000 tons in 1985), and then to slow to about 2.5% per annum through
1995.



ANNEX 9
Page 5
General Cargo
11.       General cargo traffic on the CFCO railway declined over 1969-74
from 634,000 tons to 516,000 tons (see Table 3 of the main report). This
was partly due to a gradual reduction in Chad and CAR traffic over the ATC
system in this period, stemming from a stagnation of economic development
and, in the case of Chad, from some diversion of traffic to trans-Cameroon
routes.
12.       The assessment of Congo's present economic position and growth
prospects leads to a GDP projected growth rate of over 5.0 percent per annum
in constant terms, with related growth rates of 6.0 percent for exports and
7.0% for imports. These projections, and the structure of exports and imports,
in turn suggest, under the assumption of no transport constraint, an overall
growth rate of 5.0% for general cargo going down the line to Brazzaville, and
of 7.0% for general cargo going up the line to Pointe Noire. While traffic
might be expected to grow at a slightly faster rate than GDP, a more conser-
vative approach has been taken to compensate for the decline or stagnation in
that proportion of traffic originating or terminating in CAR and Chad.
Bulk Oil Products
13.       The growth of bulk oil traffic over recent years is shown in Table 3
of the main report. From 1975 on, POL traffic is assumed to increase at 7.5%
per year, based on estimates of overall national growth, past trends, and the
present level of activity in the oil sector.
Manganese Ore
14.       Manganese ore traffic from the Moanda mine in Gabon is predictable
to a considerable extent on the basis of planned production. From the 2.0
million ton level in 1974, production will increase to 2.3 million tons in
1975 and 2.6 million tons by 1976, and is expected to maintain this volume
until 1980. Far less certain is the ability of the Transgabon railway now
being built from Libreville to handle, among other goods, that portion of the
planned output of the Moanda mine which is expected during the 1980's to
exceed the capacity of the cableway linking the mine with the Congo railhead
at Mbinda. The latest available information indicates that the Transgabon
railway will not be fully operational until 1985 at the earliest. It has
therefore been assumed that the CFCO system will continue to handle annually
about 2.6 million tons of ore up to and including 1985. After 1985, the CFCO
would still be able to handle the 2 million tons of ore which it is committed
to carry under an inter-governmental agreement between Gabon and Congo concern-
ing the flow of ore traffic beyond the year 2000. However, the new Transgabon
line would be capable of handling the entire production of Moanda ore at a cost
at least equal to and possibly lower than that of the CFCO. Since one of the
objectives of the economic analysis of the project is to ensure that the pro-
posed realignment would not duplicate other investments in the Central African
region, it has been assumed for analytical purposes, that all Moanda ore would
be evacuated over the Transgabon railway commencing in 1986.

Consolidated Traffic Forecast over the )Fourastie-Loubomo Section and Regional
Country Participation
15.       The consolidated forecast of traffic relevant to the realignment
project (Fourastie-Loubomo section of the CFCO) is presented in Table 1 of
this Annex, and of traffic over the various sections of the ATC system in
Table 3 of the main report. It should be noted that an important part of this
traffic is to serve transport needs of neighboring countries. Thus, in 1978
Congo traffic will account for only 23% of the total tonnage carried by the,
CFCO over the Fourastie-Loubomo section, while Gabon will account for 62%,
CAR for 10%, Cameroon for 3%, and Chad for 2%. In 1985, because of substan-
tial increase in its timber production, Congo's participation in the traffic
will increase to 40%, and the percentages for Gabon, CAR, Cameroon and Chad
will be 42%, 10%, 6% and 1% respectively. In 1995, Congo participation in
the traffic would jump to 72% as a result of the continued increase in timber
production and the assumed diversion of manganese to Gabon railway; during
that year, Gabon would account for only 1% of the CFCO traffic, while CAR,
Cameroon and Chad would increase their share to 16%, 9% and 2% respectively.

CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
Alternatives to ATC Timber Evacuation Routes
A.  General
1.        ATC presently has an almost total monopoly for transport of timber
from the areas which it serves. The Congo itself, as well as southern Gabon
and forestry blocs 5, 6, and 11 in southeastern Cameroon (see Maps 1 and 2
attached to this Annex), depends entirely on ATC because there is no competing
infrastructure; only CAR has a limited option to evacuate through Cameroon,
but then at costs that are generally prohibitive. This pattern of evacuation
is expected to continue at least until 1985, at which time Cameroon and CAR
may open up the areas presently under ATC's exclusive influence, and Gabon is
expected to have the railway extended to Franceville. Also by 1985, capacity
of the Cameroon railway (Regifercam) and of the port of Douala will probably
be increased, and existing bottlenecks therefore removed.
B. Evacuation from Cameroon
2.        Provided the above-mentioned investments in Cameroon are made,
timber from Moloundou could move on two possible routes: (a) by road through
Yokadouma or Lomie to the Belabo railhead (about 630 km), and from there on
Regifercam to Douala (600 km); or (b) by road via Sangmelia to Mbalmayo (650 km),
and then by rail to Douala (300 km) (see details in Table 1 of this Annex). In
terms of shipping costs, however, either of these routes would be less accep-
table than ATC for the entire bloc 6, which constitutes the bulk of the timber
to be carried by ATC; similarly, most of the timber in blocs 5 and 11, and in
the southeastern corner of the Yokadouina reserve (blocs 1, 2, and 3) could be
evacuated at considerable cost advantage through Pointe Noire. Furthermore,
floated timber (which contributes more than half the production of southeastern
Cameroon) could be best exploited if sent through the Congo (Tables 2 and 3).
On account of these considerations, all timber from southern Cameroon has been
retained for ATC.
C. Evacuation from CAR
3.        As an alternative to ATC transport, CAR timber could potentially be
evacuated from the Oubangui/Lobaye or Upper Sangha areas through an improved
road system leading to Berberati, and from there to Belabo for loading on the
railway to Douala (Table 4). (Relevant distances are: Bangui-Belabo, about
800 km; Salo-Belabo, about 590 km.) Small volumes of timber (sawn wood) presently
use this route. However, as in the case of timber from southeastern Cameroon,
ATC will remain highly competitive after execution of Uts 1974-78 investment plan
and long-term program, even on completion of possible infrastructure improvements
in Cameroon.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
4.        The cost advantages of tran8port on ATC are greatest for CAR's
forestry blocs 1-5 and most of 7, and only the northern part of 7, plus 6 and 8,
are approaching the future isocost evacuation line. Floated timber from all the
blocs, representing about half the projected CAR export tonnage, cannot be
economically exploited unless shipped through Pointe Noire. Also, the only
products that could be shipped through Cameroon are logs unsuited for floating
and processed timber, but this would result in considerably reduced profits
(Tables 2 and 3). In view of these considerations, all timber from forestry
blocs 1-5, 9-13, and two-thirds of 7, has been assigned to ATC; forestry blocs
6,8, and one-third of 7, are counted as using the Cameroon route.
D. Evacuation through Zaire
5.        It is considered highly improbable that significant volumes of timber
originating in ATC's zone of influence could be evacuated through Zaire. Two
principal factors lead to this conclusion:
(a) Cameroon, CAR, Gabon and Congo are economically associated in the
West African Customs and Economic Union (UDEAC), while Zaire lies out-
side this group. Consequently, administrative and physical delays in
movement of traffic by the integrated ATC system would probably be less
than if transfers had to be effected between ATC and the Zaire transport
system.
(b) The Bank-financed Kinshasa-Matadi railway improvement has a short
economic horizon to 1980, and there is not likely to be spare capacity
at the port of Matadi much beyond that date. Consequently, to handle
any additional traffic from the Congo in the period beyond 1980 when
the main benefits from the ATC investment program are expected to materi-
alize, would require either expansion of Matadi or evacuation via the
proposed new port at Banana. The latter, however, is not expected to be-
come operational for inland traffic until the late 1980's at the ear-
liest, as the initial port development will consist of facilities to
serve the needs of industrial development in the Banana area only.
Thus, for the intervening period, investments at Matadi would be re-
quired to avoid severe constraints on evacuation of Congo timber; however,
such facilities at Matadi would not remain in operation once the Banana
facilities become available. The economic benefits would, therefore, be
obtained for only a very short period, and it is a priori highly un-
likely that combined Matadi-Banana port and Kinshasa-Natadi-Banana rail-
way investments required to handle Congo traffic would be as cost-
beneficial as the proposed ATC program.
E. Evacuation through Gabon
6.        Assuming that the railway will be extended to Franceville by 1981,
Gabonese timber which presently relies on ATC will probably be diverted to a
Gabonese port. By contrast, timber from southern Congo would be retained for
Pointe Noire because of the advantages in transport costs.

ANNEX 11
