CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
Economic Evaluation
Organization of Analysis
1.        The proposed project covers the ATC investment plan for the period
1974-78, which includes investments in the CFCO railways, the port of Pointe
Noire, and river ports and services. The critical component of the project,
accounting for 62% of the ATC program, is the CFCO railway section between
Fourastie and Loubomo, where the line has reached capacity and is in very poor
and dangerous condition. The economic analysis therefore focussed on eva-
luating various investment alternatives to improve the condition of this sec-
tion. Once the optimal solution was found (realignment of the section), the
analysis was broadened to include the whole ATC program, including the realign-
ment and the complementary investments in the railway, ports and river services.
2.        The economic analysis required preparation of a forecast of trans-
port demand through the critical railway section and through the rest of the
ATC system. This forecast is presented separately in Annex 9 and in Table 3
of the main report.
3.        The effect of potential changes in critical variables with respect
to estimated values was tested through a sensitivity analysis applied to traf-
fic forecasts, railway capacity, and criteria for evaluation of benefits.
Changes in investment costs were not analyzed, however, because bids for the
main civil works contract (representing 82% of the total realignment costs)
have already been received and evaluated. Original cost estimates for the
rest of the ATC program were reviewed in the light of bidding costs for the
realignment.
4.        All cost and benefit streams are in 1974 constant prices, net of
taxes. The analysis assumes start of investments in 1976, and considers the
stream of costs and benefits up to year 2000.
PART I: ANALYSIS OF FOURASTIE-LOUBOMO ALTERNATIVES
Investment Alternatives
5.        Four alternatives were considered to approach the current critical
problem on the Fourastie-Loubomo section:
(a)  the "Proposed Solution", consisting of replacing the
existing section by construction of new realigned
section, thereby reducing maintenance and operating
costs and increasing line capacity;



ANNEX 11
Page 2
(b)  the "Reference Case", consisting of making the minimum
investments necessary to avoid further deterioration of the
section, and of accepting frustration of future traffic due
to limited line capacity;
(c)  the "Line Improvement Solution", involving repairs and
improvement of the existing section to maintain services
and relieve congestion to a limited extent; and
(d)  the "Reference Case plus Road", consisting of the above
Reference Case plus the construction of a road between
Pointe Noire and Brazzaville, to which potentially
frustrated railway traffic could be diverted.
The Proposed Solution (realignment) was tested against the three other
alternatives.
Proposed Solution vs. Reference Case
6.   This case is the closest equivalent to comparing the Proposed Solution
with the "do nothing" solution.  However, since failure of the Fourastie-
Loubomo section would be imminent if no action is taken,and the result of
the closure would be intolerable, a Reference Case consisting of the minimum
investment necessary to retain the present condition was devised as a proxy
to the "do nothing" solution. Investments under the Reference Case comprise
repairs to the Bamba tunnel and bridges (CFAF 1.60 billion), and upgrading
of the signalling and telecommunications systems (CFAF 0.30 billion).
7.        The economic evaluation of this case considers as costs the net
economicinvestment costs of the Proposed Solution minus those of the Refer-
ence Case, amounting to CFAF 19.76 billion (CFAF 21.66 billion - CFAF 1.90
billion). The annual costs would be as follows:
in CFAF billion at 1974 prices
1976                           5.88
1977                           6.41
1978                           5.26
1979                           2.21
8.        The benefits to be derived from the Proposed Solution are two-fold:
(a) savings in railway operating costs due to improved line characteristics



ANNEX 11
Page 3
and operating conditions (see Table 1) 1/, and (b) avoidance of traffic frus-
tration which would have occurred in the Reference Case.
9.        The quantification of benefits from frustrated traffic uses the
traffic forecast given in Annex 9. Traffic demand exceeding the capacity
of the railway will be frustrated because there is no alternative means of
transport. Because of the normally high value of general cargo, and the
obligation of CFCO to give priority to manganese traffic, it has been assumed
that all frustrated traffic will be timber (the largest traffic growth assumed
in the forecast) emanating from south and north Congo, the Central African
Republic (CAR), and southeastern Cameroon. For the sake of adopting a conser-
vative assumption regarding feasibility of the Proposed Solution, two other
large traffic components -- Gabonese manganese ore, as well as timber -- are
assumed to be diverted from the CFCO to the Transgabon Railway upon completion
of the latter, expected in 1985(see Annex 9, para. 14). The benefits to
COMILOG from the Proposed Solution compared to the Reference Case (that is,
savings in fuel, personnel, rolling stock and locomotives, and maintenance)
are relatively small -- estimated at a maximum of CFAF 44 million for a traf-
fic of 2.6 million tons 2/ -- and are not considered in the economic analysis.
Frustrated timber is evaluated as the value-added loss to the regional economy,
and is quantified as 50% of the profit (net of tax) derived from the timber
produced plus taxes levied by the countries involved. Attachment I to this
Annex presents basic data about production and transport costs, f.o.b. prices,
the relative importance of various types of wood, and tax rates applied in
Cameroon, CAR and Congo; this shows that the average value added for timber is
as follows 3/:
in CFAF per ton
Congo North                        6,300
Cameroon                           6,600
CAR                                6,250
1/   Unless otherwise noted, Table numbers refer to Tables of this Annex.
2/   According to a BCEOM study on the savings to COMILOG from the realign-
ment of the CFCO; COMILOG operates its own locomotives and freight
wagons for the transport of manganese over the CFCO line.
3/   These figures, based on 1974 prices, are used throughout the analysis;
since FOB prices of West African hardwoods increased substantially in
1975 and continuous increases (in constant terms) are expected until
1985, the analysis conservatively does not take into account potential
increases in unit value added.



ANNEX 11
Page 4
10.       The amount of frustrated traffic, given the forecast traffic demand,
depends on the capacity of the line and on train requirements resulting from
technical characteristics and from a decision on traffic priorities. A capa-
city of 13.55 train paths per day per direction 1/ has been estimated for the
Reference Case from mid-1979 onwards, on the basis of an efficiency factor of
0.65, a peak traffic factor of 0.9, and an average speed of 30 km/h (plus the
necessary time for crossing and signalling) for the critical segment of the
Fourastie-Loubomo section (Les Bandas-Moukonda). This capacity implies an
improvement over the present daily capacity of 11.75 train paths per direction,
and would be difficult to reach, even with increased technical assistance.
Assuming that the improvement is gradual, capacity in 1978 (once work under
the Reference Case would have been completed) would be only 12.00 train paths
per day per direction. However, during execution of the repairs to the existing
line under the Reference Case, the line capacity would be 10.75 train paths/
day/direction in 1976, and is assumed to increase to 11.00 in 1977.
I1.       The capacity of the line in the Proposed Solution is estimated at
15.95 train paths per day per direction based on an average speed of 40 km/h
for the critical section on the new alignment, and an efficiency factor of
0.77. The efficiency is assumed to increase following the realignment because
of improved performance resulting from intensive training, better train control
by dispatchers, safer train operations due to signalling improvements, and
reduced requirements of track maintenance.
12.       The number of freight trains required to carry the forecast traffic
is calculated as follows: (a) converting annual traffic forecast to train
requirements by dividing by the average net load of the appropriate train
type; (b) assuming timber, manganese,and POL trains return empty, and adjust-
ing the number of empty trains for the irregular flow of traffic up and down
the line; and (c) assuming that one service train and one local train per day/
direction are required, and that the two passenger trains currently in service
will be maintained as priority services.
13.       The comparison of line capacity with train requirements yields the
amount of traffic frustration shown in Table 2. The corresponding benefit
stream for the frustrated traffic, together with the operating costs savings
and the net investment costs are shown in Table 3. The rate of return obtained
for the Proposed Solution was 18%.
14.       Because the rate of return depends greatly on the magnitude of traffic
frustration, a sensitivity analysis was conducted adopting alternative assump-
tions regarding operating efficiency and train requirements. Three alternative
reference cases were thus derived. Under Alternative Reference Case A, it was
assumed that operating efficiency would improve immediately (11.00 train paths/
direction/day during 1976-1977, and 13.55 from 1978 onwards), and that only one
1/   The time required for one train path on any given section of the line
depends on: length of the section, train speed, time required for
stopping and acceleration, and time required for signalling procedures.



ANNEX 11
Page 5
passenger train would be run per day. Timber traffic would substitute for the
eliminated passenger train, but some 25% of total passenger traffic would be
diverted to air transport at a higher cost. Under Alternative Reference Case
B, it was also assumed that only one passenger train would be run per day, but
the current level of operating efficiency was retained. In Alternative Refer-
ence Case C, current level of operating efficiency and current passenger ser-
vice priority (two trains daily) were adopted. The rates of return obtained
for the Proposed Solution compared with the above alternative reference cases
were 10% in Case A, 19% in Case B, and 34% in Case C.1/.
15.       As explained in para. 9, quantification of benefits from frustrated
traffic was done on a regional basis, that is, considering the value added
loss to the Congo, Cameroon, and CAR of frustrated timber traffic. The ana-
lysis assumed further that all available manganese traffic would be carried
by the CFCO. An alternative evaluation of frustrated traffic was conducted
to determine the "national" rather than the "regional" economic return of the
Proposed Solution. In the national analysis, it was assumed that the CFCO
would carry a minimum of 2.0 million tons of manganese through 1985 (according to
the agreement between Congo and Gabon), and would only carry more than this
tonnage if capacity were available on the railway after all timber traffic
demand had been met, because CFCO net revenues from Cameroon and CAR
timber are substantially higher than from manganese; then, benefits on frus-
trated traffic were computed as net revenue loss to the railway from manganese
and Cameroon and CAR timber, and as value added loss for Congo timber (see
Table 4). In the case of manganese, the revenue loss includes, in addition
to the normal tariff, the bonus of CFAF 300 per ton which COMILOG pays to
the CFCO for all manganese carried over and above 2.2 million tons per year.
The rates of return obtained were 9% for the Proposed Solution vs. the Refe-
rence Case, and 7%, 12%, and 15% for the Proposed Solution compared to Alter-
native Cases A, B and C, respectively.
16.       As the project is to be financed on concessionary terms by grants
and soft loans that will be available because of the regional importance of
the project but would not be available for other projects in the Congo, a
further variation of the economic analysis evaluated the "national" economic
return of shadow pricing the investment costs according to the grant component
of the available financing. Under this hypothesis, the rate of return for the
Proposed Solution vs. the Reference Case and Alternative Reference Cases A, B,
and C was 12%, 10%, 15% and 19%, respectively. The assumption that Cameroon/
CAR timber could be shipped using a route other than the CFCO from 1985 was
also tested in the "regional" evaluation, and yielded rates of return for the
1/   Insofar as the economic analysis is based on a comparison of the Proposed
Solution with the Reference Case, and the major benefit to the Proposed
Solution is the avoidance of traffic frustration resulting from railway
capacity constraints in the Reference Case, the comparison of the Proposed
Solution with Alternative Reference Cases (differing in the amount of
traffic carried) serves at the same time as a proxy to a sensitivity ana-
lysis to potential changes in the traffic forecast.



ANNEX 11
Page 6
Proposed Solution compared to the Reference Case and Alternative Reference
Cases A, B and C, of 13%, 7%, 16% and 32%, respectively (Table 5); in this
analysis, very similar results would be obtained from the national viewpoint,
because frustrated foreign traffic would only exist for a few years. As the
rate of return falls to a marginal value only under Alternative Reference
Case A (which is considered a highly hypothetical case), the conclusion to
be derived from this analysis is that the Proposed Solution is a clearly
better economic option than the Reference Case, and that it would be a prefer-
able option even if it were considered from a narrow national viewpoint only.
Proposed Solution vs. Line Improvement Case
17.       A second alternative to the Proposed Solution (realignment), after
the "do nothing" or Reference Case, is to improve the existing section between
Fourastie and Loubomo. This option includes major civil works as detailed
below; construction is assumed to be spread over four years to minimize dis-
ruption of traffic.

18.       During construction of the above improvements, capacity of the line
is estimated to be 10 train paths per day per direction. Following completion
of the improvements, a line capacity of 14.6 would take place on the assumption
that the efficiency factor on the line could be increased to 0.7 from the cur-
rent 0.6. A further assumption adopted in the analysis is that passenger ser-
vice would be limited to one train per day each way in order to maximize timber
traffic. Incremental costs and benefits of the Proposed Solution versus the
Line Improvement Case are presented in Table 6. The rate of return of the
Proposed Solution versus the Line Improvement Case under these conditions is
estimated at 20%, showing that the small additional investment (CFAF 4.6 bil-
lion) required to go from this proposed solution to the improvement solution is
fully justified. In addition, it should be noted that with the line improve-
ment case, there will be very little room for subsequent increases in capacity,
as (a) the distances between stations are already small, and (b) the technical
characteristics of the line will still cause a difficult operating situation
and limitations in efficiency. Any shortfall in productivity increases or
traffic growth higher than those anticipated in this analysis would therefore
result in substantial traffic frustration, and would correspondingly increase
the advantage of the proposed alternative over the improvement alternative.



ANNEX 11
Page 7
Proposed Solution vs. Reference Case plus Road
19.       The third alternative project to the Proposed Solution (realign-
ment) was a combinaton of the Reference Case plus the construction of a
road from Brazzaville to Pointe Noire. Parts of this route already exist
to varying standards of construction, but improvements, reconstruction or
construction would be required over most of its length in order to meet
freight traffic requirements. A variant to this rail/road combination,
consisting of the construction of a road section between Fourastie and
Loubomo only, was discarded a priori as this would be the most expensive
section to build (over a mountainous area), and because of the high trans-
shipment costs that would be involved at both terminal points.
20.       The analysis with the road assumed that three types of traffic
would divert from the railway to the road: (a) passenger traffic -- 50%
of total passenger transport demand would shift to road transport; (b)
general cargo -- 25% of total general cargo would move voluntarily to the
road transport; (c) general cargo -- additional cargo would be forced to
shift to road transport whenever total traffic demand (after discounting
voluntary diversion) exceeded railway capacity. This distribution of
traffic between the modes is based on comparative costs and service charac-
teristics. A higher diversion rate is considered unlikely at present in
view of the long-haul nature of most of the general cargo (transit traffic
of CAR and Chad, and import/export traffic of Brazzaville), and the
social requirement to maintain a minimum passenger service on the railway.
Because of transport capacity provided by the road, no traffic frustration
would take place in this case once the road is open.
21.       In the economic evaluation of the Proposed Solution versus the rail/
road combination, costs considered were the net investment costs (Proposed
Solution minus Reference Case costs and road construction costs), and the
reduction in transport costs for passengers diverting from the railway to the
road. Benefits for the Proposed Solution were the road maintenance costs
attributable to diverted railway traffic, operating cost savings on diverted
general cargo, railway operating cost savings for the non-diverted traffic,
and frustrated timber traffic during construction of the railway improvements
in the Reference Case.  A summary of the methodology used to calculate costs
and benefits is presented in Table 7.
22.       Road construction costs were estimated for the normal Reference
Case and for Alternative Reference Cases A, B and C (see para. 14) according
to the traffic generated to each alternative. In Alternative Reference Case A
under which traffic of about 100 trucks and buses per day would develop, cons-
truction costs of the road were estimated at CFAF 14.0 billion, net of taxes.
In Alternative Reference Case C, with some 400 heavy vehicles per day, con-
struction costs were estimated at CFAF 19.3 billion. For the normal Reference
Case and Alternative Reference Case B having an intermediate traffic between
that of Cases A and C, construction costs of CFAF 16.7 billion were assumed.



ANNEX 11
Page 8
23.       The cost and benefit streams of the Proposed Solution compared to
the Reference Case plus the road are shown in Table 8. It can be appreciated
that the net investment costs are relatively small (because of the high con-
struction costs of the road), and that they are largely offset by the benefits
accruing to the Proposed Solution due to frustrated traffic during the period
when improvements are carried out in the railway reference case. If the proj-
ect opening date is assumed to be in mid-1979, the rate of return for the
Proposed Solution would be 43% or 22%, depending on whether 100% or 80% of
road costs are considered. An alternative opening date of both the road and
the railway realignment in 1983 1/ yields a 15% rate of return or the Proposed
Solution if 80% of road construction costs were allocated to diverted railway
traffic (thus allowing for additional traffic to generate on the road), and a
27% return if the full road costs are considered.
24.       Using the same alternative reference cases described in para. 14,
the rate of return for the Proposed Solution compared to the combination of
the road plus Alternative Reference Cases A, B, or C was 9%, 17%, and infinite
respectively when 80% of road costs were considered, and 12%, 24%, and infinite
respectively, when full road costs were assumed. The analysis showed, there-
fore, that realignment of the Fourastie-Loubomo railway section is a better eco-
nomic decision than the combination of modest improvements in the railway and
construction of a road, mainly due to the high cost of road construction. It
should be noted that because the analysis with the road assumes no traffic
frustration (except during the repairs to the railway line while the road
is under construction), the national rate of return would be very similar to
the regional one, and therefore this conclusion is fully valid under both
viewpoints.
PART II: ANALYSIS OF TOTAL ATC INVESTMENT PROGRAM
25.       The ATC Investment Program for the period 1974-78 consists, in
addition to the proposed realignment of the railway, of other CFCO invest-
ments, as well as investments in river ports and services and in the Port
of Pointe Noire. The economic investment costs of the total ATC investment
program (net of takes and including physical contingencies) were derived
from Table 9 of the main report.
26.       The analysis of the total ATC program followed the same methodology
adopted to study the Fourastie-Loubomo section, that is, to compare costs and
benefits of the proposed investments with those of a reference case, and to
compute incremental costs and benefits resulting therefrom. The incremental
economic investment costs of the whole ATC program, compared to the appro-
priate reference case for investments in the CFCO (excluding realignment),
ports and river services, amount to CFAF 2.14 billion. Investments in the
CFCO and the Port of Pointe Noire in the proposed investment program are the
1/   Year when traffic frustration would start under Alternative Reference
Case A, the most unfavorable situation to the Proposed Solution when
compared to the Road plus Reference Case alternative.

the reference case traffic (a major part of these investments in fact have
been implemented or are underway) which is essential to the Congo (see Table 9
of this Annex). Incremental investments occur therefore only in river ports
and services. Disbursement of the incremental investment costs was assumed to

study of the realignment, in order to test the effect of different railway
traffic levels on the feasibility of investments in river ports and services.

SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
ATC - Ongoing Capital Expenditures
The items mentioned below are included in the capital expenditure
programs for ATC under all cases considered (proposed solution, major invest-
ments and reference case). They constitute either the continuation of projects
already started, or those for which funds are already committed. They thus
do not affect the comparative analyses through which the proposed solution
is justified.
I. Port of Pointe Noire
(a) Berths. Completion of ongoing contract for manganese and
general cargo berths (1975).
(b) Dredging. Capital dredging around breakwater where sand now
encroaches on access channel (1975-78).
(c) Equipment. Barges (some for dredger for work under (b) above),
service tug and other small items (1975-77).
(d) Slipway. Completion of ongoing contract (1975).
(e) Workshops. Completion of ongoing contract (1975).
(f) Miscellaneous items (1975-78).
II. CFCO
(a) Track works. Completion of ongoing works (1975).
(b) Marshalling yards. Completion of ongoing works (1975-76).
(c) Signalling and telecommunications. Completion of ongoing
contract (1975-76).
(d)  Workshops and depot.  Completion of ongoing works (1975).
III. River Ports
(a) Brazzaville port. Completion of ongoing extension works
(1975-78).
(b) Secondary river ports. Completion of ongoing extension works
(1975-76).



ANNEX 11
Attachment 2
Page 2
(c)  W,rkshops.  Completion of ongoing works (1975).
(d)  Equipment.  Delivery of equipment already ordered (1975).
IV. River Services
(a) River navigation. Improvements to river channels to lengthen
season available for traffic (197.5-76).
(b) Flaig c-graft. Delivery of tugs and barges committed under
ADB loan (1975-76).

CONGO
SECOND RAILWAY PROJECT
Sensitivity Analysis of Financial Forecasts
1.        A sensitivity analysis of the financial forecasts was conducted to test
the impact of a possible decrease in ATC's cash generation and/or an increase
in the cost of capital investments on ATC's ability to: (a) finance its opera-
ting costs; (b) progressively restore its working capital and then maintain it
at an adequate level; (c) service its debt; and (d) finance its capital in-
vestments and renewals. The analysis was carried out assuming the combined
effect of a 20% decrease in gross operating revenue generated by the timber
traffic, a 10% increase in working expenditures, and a 10% increase in the
cost of capital investments excluding the CFCO realignment (the cost of which
is based on firm bid prices increased by physical and price contingencies).
2.        The sensitivity analysis reviews the same two periods considered in
the main financial analysis, namely, 1976-79 covering the construction period
of the CFCO realignment, and 1980-86 assuming full utilization of the realign-
ment. Table 1 of this Annex shows the yearly impact of the sensitivity assump-
tion on ATC's cash position, assuming ATC is granted the same external financial
support as in the main analysis (para. 6.10). Since it was agreed that ATC
would not have to provide any funds for the CFCO realignment during its con-
struction (in view of its financial situation and its contributions to the re-
mainder of the 1974-78 investment plan), the table does not show the cost of the
realignment nor its external financing; however, since ATC is committed to
service loans contracted for constructing the realignment, the calculations used
include the relevant debt service. The table on Page 2 su-marizes the sources
and applications of funds shown in the attached Table 1 but includes the cost
and financing of the CFCO realignment so that ATC's cash flow under the sensi-
tivity assumption conforms with that shown for the main analysis.

ANNEX 12
Page 3
3.        The above table shows that under the sensitivity assumption, ATC would
generate enough funds to finance its working expenses and restore and maintain
its working capital during both periods considered. However, ATC would be short
about CFAF 9 billion out of the CFAF 20 billion required to finance its share
of the Investment Plan and its debt service in 1976-79, and about CFAF 37 billion
out of the CFAF 96 billion required to finance its investments and debt ser-
vice during 1980-86.
4.        Although the Government has agreed to compensate for any shortage of
funds of ATC for implementing the Investment Plan and/or servicing its debts
during 1976-80, it has been assumed that the Government would not provide
further funds to ATC beyond its already heavy commitments in financing the
Investment Plan and providing financial support to ATC during 1976-80. ATC
would, therefore, have to compensate any partial or total materialization of
the sensitivity analysis assumptions by increasing its tariffs above those
ass=zed for the main analysis and, in case the ceiling of such increases would
be attained, to have recourse to additional borrowing. Calculations showed
that ATC could overcome the maximum shortage of funds mentioned in para. 3
above by implementing the following measures: (a) increase tariffs by 15%
instead of the 0l forecasted in the main analysis for 1978; (b) borrow up to
CFAF 8 billion (US$35 million equivalent) in addition to the borrowing pro-
gram used in the main analysis for the Investment Plan; (c) increase tariffs
by 12% instead of 10% every two years through 1980-86; and (d) borrow up to
40% of its investments planned for 1980-86. The tariff increases mentioned
under (a) and (c) above would bring ATC's tariffs roughly in line with fore-
cast inflation. These tariff increases cannot well be exceeded as ATC has
already increased its rates by 75% between February 1973 and March 1976, and
the future increases will be borne mostly by the timber traffic which will
generate about one-third and one-half of ATC's forecast revenue in 1978 and
1986 respectively. The additional borrowing would either have to be on very
soft terms or include capitalization of interest.
5.        The table on Page 4 summarizes the cumulated projected tables of
ATC's sources and applications of funds for 1976-79 and 1980-86 under the
assumptions described in para. 4 above.

6.        ATC's balance sheet as of December 31, 1986 resulting from application
of the sensitivity-hypothesis and assumptions described in paras. 3 and 4 of
this Annex can be summarized as follows:
(in CFAF billion)
Current assets                             19
Current liabilities                         8
New fixed assets                          219
Loan capital                               52
Equity equivalent                         178
The above reflects an acceptable financial position characterized by : (a) a
balanced cash position; (b) satisfactory working capital coverage (para. 6.09
of the main report); and (c) a satisfactory 29/71 debt to equity position which
shows that ATC could comfortably face by 1987 the annual CFAF 3.5 billion increase
in its debt service which would result from the additional borrowing assumed in
paras. 3 and 4 above.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
7.        It can be concluded from the above that ATC could withstand the
effects of the sensitivity assumptions materializing, provided that tariffs
are increased and soft-term borrowing is available as required. For ATC, it
would result in: (a) a substantial increase in the long-term debt; and (b)
a delay in paying dividends to the Government. Any alleviation of the indi-
vidual components of the sensitivity assumption would ease ATC's financial
position as presented in para. 2.
